Does an Unqualified Proposition Imply Actuality?
Debate on whether an unqualified proposition implies actuality of the relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the debate over whether an unqualified proposition (mutlaqa) necessarily implies actuality of the relation, and identify counterexamples from common usage.

Core Idea
An unqualified proposition is named 'mutlaqa' because actuality is commonly understood, but it is not a logical necessity.

In this passage, the commentator explores the meaning of the term 'mutlaqa' (unqualified proposition). The initial claim is that when a proposition lacks an explicit modality, it is taken to assert actuality of the relation. However, an objection is raised: custom and language do not always support this, as seen in essential predications. The passage refines our understanding of how modal qualifiers work in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
Find the first position in the passage: when a proposition is unqualified, actuality of the relation is understood, so it is called mutlaqa.
The naming is a metonymy: the signified (the actual relation) is named after the signifier (the unqualified utterance).
2
Find the objection
Locate the counterargument: it is not necessary that the meaning is actuality; the relation could be in the mode of possibility. The example 'Every fire is cold' is given as a case where the relation is not actual.
The objection is reinforced by citing Shaykh Yasin, who argues that common examples like 'Every human is an animal' do not convey actuality.
3
Examine the response
See how the commentator reconciles: the naming is a general direction (tawjih), meaning actuality is understood in general, even if not in every specific case.
The response acknowledges exceptions but retains the conventional naming.
4
Assess the logical status
Determine whether the passage treats actuality connotation as a logical necessity or a conventional understanding. Conclude it is the latter.
This distinction prevents overgeneralizing the meaning of unqualified propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يُفهم منها فعليَّةُ النّسبةِ، فسُمِّيَتِ القضيَّةُ الَّتي حكم فيها بفعليَّة النسبة مطلقة؛ تسمية للمدلول بِاسْمِ الدَّالّ . والحاصلُ : أَنَّهُ ليسَ بِلازم أنْ يكونَ مَعنى القضيَّةِ إذا لم تذكرِ الجهةُ أنَّ النِّسبةَ ثابتة بالفعل؛ لجواز أنْ يكونَ ثُبوت المحمول للموضوع على سبيل الإمكان، كَقولِكَ : كلَّ نارٍ باردةٌ . وفي الشيخ ياسين ما نصه: (قولُهُ: لأنَّ القضيَّةَ إِذا أُطلِقَتْ ... إلخ) فيه : أنَّ هذا لا يصحُ كُلِّيًّا؛ إذ لا يفهم العرفُ واللُّغةُ مِن مثل قولنا : «كل إنسان حيوان»، وزيدٌ قائمٌ أَوْ يقومُ» فعليَّة النِّسبة . وقد يُجابُ : بأنَّ هذا توجيه لِلتّسمية في الجملة؛ أي: يُفهم ذلك في الجملة ولو في بعض الأفراد.
Actuality of the relation is understood from it, so the proposition that is judged with actuality of the relation is called 'mutlaqa' — naming the signified by the name of the signifier. The conclusion: it is not necessary that the meaning of the proposition when the modality is not mentioned is that the relation is actual; for it is possible that the subject's having the predicate is in the mode of possibility, like your saying: 'Every fire is cold.' And in al-Shaykh Yasin is his text: (His saying: 'Because the proposition when unqualified... etc.') In it: that this is not universally valid; for custom and language do not understand from like our saying: 'Every human is an animal', and 'Zayd is standing' or 'stands' actuality of the relation. And it may be answered: that this is a direction for the naming in general; i.e., it is understood in general, even if in some individuals.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange about the unqualified proposition (al-mutlaqa). It opens by stating that when a proposition is unqualified (i.e., without explicit modality), the actuality (al-fi'liyya) of the relation between subject and predicate is understood. Consequently, such a proposition is called 'mutlaqa' because it signifies actuality — a metonymic transfer from the signifier (the uttered proposition) to the signified (the actual relation). The commentator then raises an objection: it is not necessary that an unqualified proposition always implies actuality; the relation could be in the mode of possibility (bi al-imkan), as in the example 'Every fire is cold' (which is actually impossible, but meant as a possible judgment in the sense of 'it could be cold' — a logical possibility). The objection is strengthened by citing Shaykh Yasin, who argues that the claim is not universal; for example, 'Every human is an animal' or 'Zayd is standing' do not, in common custom and language, convey actuality of the relation in the sense of temporal existence, but rather a habitual or essential predication. The response to this objection is that the naming is a general direction (tawjih) — it holds 'in general' or 'in some individuals', meaning that the actuality connotation is a prevailing understanding but not an absolute rule. The passage thus refines the definition of the mutlaqa proposition by acknowledging exceptions while maintaining the validity of the conventional naming.

Key Terms
مطلقة
الجهة
فعليَّة النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the discussion about the unqualified proposition, what does the example 'Every human is an animal' demonstrate?
A) That all unqualified propositions imply actuality of the relation.
B) That in custom and language, such propositions do not necessarily convey actuality.
C) That the proposition 'Every human is an animal' is impossible.
D) That the unqualified proposition is always named after the signified.

Key Takeaway
The unqualified proposition is conventionally associated with actuality, but this is not a logical necessity; it is a general naming convention that can admit exceptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify two Islamic science examples where an unqualified proposition might be taken to imply actuality but could also be interpreted as possibility. Explain the difference.
?
In the passage, the objection uses 'Every human is an animal' and 'Zayd is standing'. Classify each as a case where actuality is or is not understood according to custom and language, and justify.

Defining the General Possible Proposition (al-Mumkinah al-'Ammah)
Author defines al-mumkinah al-'ammah via absence of necessity of the opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the general possible proposition (al-mumkinah al-'ammah) in terms of the negation of necessity from the opposite side or negation of impossibility from the agreeing side, and distinguish it from an actual judgment.

Core Idea
The general possible proposition negates necessity, not affirms possibility.

In this passage, commentators unpack the matn's definition of the 'general possible proposition' as 'absence of necessity of the opposite.' This subtle logical notion distinguishes possible propositions from actual judgments and has two equivalent interpretations: negating necessity from the opposite side or negating impossibility from the agreeing side.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: al-mumkinah al-'ammah (general possible proposition). Note that it is a modal proposition.
The definition builds on the matn's phrase: 'or by absence of necessity of its opposite'.
2
Extract the genus
Identify the broader category: it is a proposition (qadiyya) qualified by a modality (jiha). The commentator clarifies that 'absence of necessity of the opposite' is not itself a modality but a concomitant of general possibility.
The modality here is general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm), which includes both necessary and contingent relations.
3
Extract the differentia
Find what distinguishes general possibility: negation of necessity from the side opposite the judgment. If the judgment is affirmative, the opposite is negation; if negative, the opposite is affirmation. So the proposition does not assert the predicate; it only denies that the opposite is necessary.
This is expressed as 'absence of necessity of the opposite' (adam darurat khilafiha).
4
Understand the two equivalent interpretations
The text presents two equivalent definitions: (1) negation of essential necessity from the opposing side, and (2) negation of essential impossibility from the agreeing side. The equivalence holds in terms of actuality, not logical co-extensiveness as per al-'Isam.
For example, 'Man is a writer with general possibility' means it is not necessary that man is not a writer (i.e., writing is not impossible).
5
Note the key nuance regarding judgment
Recognize that a general possible proposition does not contain an actual judgment of affirmation or negation about the predicate. The judgment is only about the modality (negation of necessity). As Mir Abu al-Fath states, it is a 'potential proposition, not actual.'
This avoids the seeming contradiction that the proposition would otherwise involve a judgment about something that may not be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ بِعَدَمِ ضَرُورَةِ خِلَافِهَا)؛ أي: إن لم يكن الحكم بضرورة النّسبة ولا بدوامها ولا بِفِعْلِيَّتها. الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ بِعَدِمٍ) عطفٌ على بضرورة، وقوله: (خلافها)؛ أي: النسبة. واعلَمْ أنَّ هذا ؛ أي: عدم ضرورة خلافِ النِّسبة؛ ليسَ كيفيَّةً، وإِنَّما هو تابع للكيفية الَّتي هِيَ الإمكانُ العام الصَّادقُ بوجوبِ ثبوتِ المحمول للموضوع وجوازِهِ، فَعدمُ ضرورةِ خلافِها حكم لازم للحكم على النّسبةِ بِالإمكان العام . انتهى عدوي، وتأمَّله. وقولُهُ: (أو بعدم ... إلخ) ، اعلم أنَّ الإمكان العام هو سَلْبُ الضَّرورةِ عَنِ الجانب المخالف للحكم بمعنى النِّسبةِ، وهذا يرجعُ له قول المصنفِ : عدم ضرورةِ خلافِ النِّسبةِ ؛ لأنَّ النِّسَبةَ هي الجانب الموافق للحكم، وخلافها هو الجانب المخالِفُ، فَعدمُ ضرورةِ خلافِ النِّسبةِ هو نَفْسُ سَلْبِ الضَّرورةِ عَنِ الجانب المخالفِ للحكم، فإذا كانَ الموافقُ إيجاباً ؛ كان المخالف سلباً، وَبِالعكس، وإذا سُلِبَتِ الضرورة عن الجانب المخالفِ ؛ كانَ ثبوتُ الموافقِ صادقاً بالوجوب والجواز. العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّهَا أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْوُجُوْدِيَّةِ اللَّا دَائِمَةِ ... إِلَخ) لا وجه للاقتصار عليهما ؛ لأنَّها أعمُ مِنَ البسائط الأربع أيضاً، وفي الدَّوَّاني : نقل عن بعضهم أنَّها ليسَتْ أعم من المشروطة العامة، وأطال في ردّه. (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ بِعَدَمِ ضَرُورَةِ خِلَافِهَا) قالَ المصنِّفُ في شرحِ الرِّسالةِ : الأولى أنْ يُقالَ تفسيرها : إنَّها التي حكم فيها بثبوتِ المحمول للموضوع أو سلبِهِ عنه؛ مع أنَّ نَقيضُ الحُكْمِ ليسَ بِضروري ؛ لكنَّه لَمَّا قصد بيان معنى الإمكان العام؛ اقتصر على ما ذكره .ا . هـ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ الإمكان العام يُفسَّرُ تارةٌ بسلب الضّرورةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ عن الجانب المخالف للحكم كما ذكر، وتارةً بسلب الامتناع الذاتي عن الجانب الموافق، فإنَّ إمكان الإيجاب معناه ؛ عدم امتناع الإيجاب أو عدم ضرورةِ السَّلْبِ، وَكَذَا الحال في إمكانِ السَّلْبِ، والتَّعبيرانِ مُتساويانِ كَمَا في السَّيِّدِ، وبحثَ فِيهِ العصامُ بأنَّ سلب الامتناع الذاتي عن الجانب الموافق وإن استلزم سلبَ الضَّرورةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ عن الجانب المخالفِ وبالعكس؛ لكنَّهُمَا لا يتصادقانِ إِلَّا أَنْ يُرادَ التّساوي بحسبِ التَّحقُّقِ دونَ الصدقِ المتعارفِ في نسب التَّصوراتِ .ا.هـ. وإنَّما كانَ التَّساوي بحسبِ التَّحقُّقِ هنا ؛ لأنَّ ضَرورَةً أحدِ الطَّرفين يَستلزم امتناعَ الآخَرِ ، فَعدمُها يستلزم عدمَهُ . (قَوْلُهُ : بَلْ يَكُوْنُ الْحُكْمُ ... إلخ) يتراءى منهُ أنَّ في القضيَّة الممكنة حكماً بالإيجاب أو السَّلْبِ، وقد عرفتَ أنْ لَا حُكْمَ فيها ، فَلْيُحْمَلِ الحُكْمُ على الحكم الموهوم نظراً إلى ظاهر العبارة .ا.هـ. عصام، ولذلك قال مير أبو الفتح : إنَّ الممكنةً العامَّةَ قَضيَّةُ بالقوَّةِ لا بالفعل، وبيانُهُ أنَّا إذا قُلْنَا : الإنسانُ كاتبُ بِالإمكان العام؛ فليس الحُكْمُ فيها إلَّا بِسَلبِ الضَّرورةِ عن الجانب المخالفِ، وأمَّا الحُكمُ في الجانب الموافقِ؛ فَلَمْ يتعرَّض لهُ حتَّى يحتمل أن يكون واقعاً وأن لا يكون ا.هـ. وقال عبد الحكيم : إنَّ الممكنةَ مُشتملة على الحكم باعتبار الجهة؛ لا بحسب ذاتها . (قَوْلُهُ : إِذِ السَّلْبُ خِلافُ النِّسْبَةِ قالَ شارحُ الغَرَّةِ : الشَّيءُ إذا لم يكن مُخالفه ضروريا ; فَنفسُهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ ضروريا ; فحينئذ تصدقُ قضيَّةٌ ضروريَّةٌ مُوافقةٌ لِمَفهوم
Or by the absence of necessity of its opposite; meaning: if the judgment is not by necessity of the relation nor by its permanence nor by its actuality. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'Or by absence') is conjuncted to 'by necessity', and his saying: ('its opposite') meaning the relation. Know that this, i.e., the absence of necessity of the opposite of the relation, is not a modality, rather it is subordinate to the modality which is general possibility, which is true by obligation of the subject having the predicate and its permissibility. So absence of necessity of its opposite is a necessary concomitant for the judgment on the relation by general possibility. End of 'Adawi, and ponder it. And his saying: (Or by absence... etc.), know that general possibility is the negation of necessity from the side opposite to the judgment in the sense of the relation. And this refers to the author's saying: 'absence of necessity of the opposite of the relation', because the relation is the side agreeing with the judgment, and its opposite is the side opposing it. So absence of necessity of the opposite of the relation is exactly the negation of necessity from the side opposing the judgment. So if the agreeing side is affirmation, then the opposing side is negation, and vice versa. And when necessity is negated from the opposing side, then the affirmation of the agreeing side is true by obligation and permissibility. Al-'Attar: (His saying: 'Because it is more general than the existential non-permanent... etc.') There is no reason to confine it to these two because it is also more general than the four simple propositions. And in al-Dawwani: it is reported from some that it is not more general than the general conditional, and he elaborated in refuting it. (His saying: 'Or by absence of necessity of its opposite') The author said in his commentary on al-Risalah: it is better to say its interpretation: it is that in which the subject having the predicate or its negation is judged, while the opposite of the judgment is not necessary. But since he intended to explain the meaning of general possibility, he limited himself to what he mentioned. Then general possibility is interpreted sometimes as negation of essential necessity from the side opposing the judgment as mentioned, and sometimes as negation of essential impossibility from the side agreeing. For the possibility of affirmation means: the impossibility of affirmation is absent or the necessity of negation is absent. Similarly for the possibility of negation. And the two expressions are equivalent as in al-Sayyid. Al-'Isam investigated it saying that negation of essential impossibility from the agreeing side, although it entails negation of essential necessity from the opposing side and vice versa, yet they are not co-extensive except if equivalence is meant in terms of actuality, not in the conventional truth in the relations of concepts. The equivalence in terms of actuality is so because necessity of one side entails impossibility of the other, so its absence entails its absence. (His saying: 'Rather the judgment is... etc.') It appears from it that in the possible proposition there is a judgment of affirmation or negation, but you have known that there is no judgment in it, so let the judgment be interpreted as the supposed judgment in view of the apparent expression. End of 'Isam. Therefore Mir Abu al-Fath said: the general possible proposition is a potential proposition, not actual. Its clarification: when we say: 'Man is a writer with general possibility', the judgment therein is only the negation of necessity from the opposing side. As for the judgment on the agreeing side, it is not addressed, so it may be real or not. And 'Abd al-Hakim said: the possible proposition includes a judgment in terms of modality, not in terms of its essence. (His saying: 'Since negation is the opposite of the relation') The commentator of al-Ghurrah said: if a thing's opposite is not necessary, then the thing itself is either necessary, in which case a necessary proposition agreeing with the concept is true.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage defines the 'general possible proposition' (al-mumkinah al-'ammah). The author's matn stated that a proposition can be qualified by 'absence of necessity of its opposite'. The commentary expands this definition. Al-Dasuqi explains that 'absence of necessity of the opposite' is not itself a modality but is a concomitant of general possibility, which is the modality of 'either necessary or possible' for the predicate's relation to the subject. Al-'Attar adds that general possibility can also be defined as negation of essential impossibility from the agreeing side, and the two definitions are equivalent. The passage then discusses the nature of judgment in a possible proposition: some commentators like Mir Abu al-Fath consider it potential, not actual, because the judgment is only about negating necessity from the opposite side, not about affirming or negating the predicate. The example given is 'Every fire is hot' with general possibility, meaning it is not necessary that fire is not hot (i.e., it is possible). This contrasts with the necessary proposition where the opposite is impossible.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام
الممكنة العامة
الجانب المخالف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of a general possible proposition (al-mumkinah al-'ammah)?
A) It affirms that the predicate is possible for the subject.
B) It negates necessity from the side opposite the relation.
C) It states that the predicate is necessary for the subject.
D) It denies the existence of the subject.

Key Takeaway
General possibility is defined by negating necessity from the opposite side, not by affirming any actual relation, making it a modal qualification that allows the predicate to be either necessary or contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find the modal proposition 'The world is temporally originated with general possibility' in a Kalam text (e.g., al-Sanusi's 'Umm al-Barahin). Explain why the judgment in this proposition does not affirm the world's actual temporal origination but only negates the necessity of its pre-eternity.
?
Construct your own example: take a statement about a fiqh ruling (e.g., 'Prayer is obligatory'), and express it as a general possible proposition. Explain what is being negated.

Defining General and Specific Possibility
Al-Dasuqi defines general possibility and distinguishes it from specific possibility with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define general possibility and specific possibility, and distinguish them using the example 'Every fire is hot with general possibility'.

Core Idea
General possibility negates necessity from one side; specific possibility from both sides.

This passage introduces two fundamental modal categories in Islamic logic: al-imkan al-‘amm (general possibility) and al-imkan al-khass (specific possibility). Al-Dasuqi defines them clearly: general possibility only negates the necessity of the opposite side, while specific possibility negates necessity from both sides. The example 'Every fire is hot' with general possibility illustrates that we are only saying it is not necessary that fire is not hot, not that fire is necessarily hot.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the Arabic terms for general and specific possibility in the passage. Note the definitions given by al-Dasuqi.
Terms الإمكان العام and الإمكان الخاص.
2
Contrast the two definitions
General possibility negates necessity from the opposite side only. Specific possibility negates necessity from both the opposite and the agreeing side.
3
Work through the example
Analyze 'كل نار حارة بالإمكان العام' – every fire is hot with general possibility. The judgment is that the negation of heat is not necessary. That is all that is asserted.
The opposite of the relation is the negation of heat.
4
Apply the distinction to another example
Consider 'Every human is possibly a writer' with general possibility. It negates the necessity of not being a writer, but does not assert actual writing. Compare with specific possibility: 'Every human is possibly a writer' with specific possibility would also negate the necessity of being a writer.
The agreeing side is the affirmation of writing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل يكونُ الحكم بعدم ضرورةِ خلافِ النِّسبةِ ; (فَالمُمْكِنَةُ العَامَّةُ ) . كَقولِنَا : كلُّ نارِ حارَّةٌ) بالإمكان العام، فحكم فيها بعدم ضرورة السَّلب إذِ السَّلْبُ خلافُ النِّسبةِ . قال الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : فَالْمُمْكِنَةُ الْعَامَّةُ) اعلَمُ أنَّ الإمكانَ العامَّ سَلْبُ الضَّرورةِ عَنِ الطَّرفِ المخالف، والإمكانَ الخاصَّ؛ سَلْبُ الضَّرورةِ عَن الطَّرفِ المخالفِ والطَّرفِ الموافقِ ، والطَّرفُ الموافق؛ مَا أَفادَتْهُ القضيَّةُ مِنَ النّسبةِ، والطَّرفُ المخالِفُ : هو خلافُ النِّسبة . (قَوْلُهُ : حَارَّةٌ) أي : يصح أن تكونَ حارَّةً، وصحته إذا لم يكن السَّلْبُ واجباً . (قَوْلُهُ : السَّلْبِ) أي: سلبِ الحرارة، قوله: (وَلَو لم يكنْ)؛ هِيَ تامة، وفاعلها العطار إمكانِ السَّلْبِ، والتَّعبيرانِ مُتساويانِ كَمَا في السَّيِّدِ، وبحثَ فِيهِ العصامُ بأنَّ سلب الامتناع الذاتي عن الجانب الموافق وإن استلزم سلبَ الضَّرورةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ عن الجانب المخالفِ وبالعكس؛ لكنَّهُمَا لا يتصادقانِ إِلَّا أَنْ يُرادَ التّساوي بحسبِ التَّحقُّقِ دونَ الصدقِ المتعارفِ في نسب التَّصوراتِ .ا.هـ. وإنَّما كانَ التَّساوي بحسبِ التَّحقُّقِ هنا ؛ لأنَّ ضَرورَةً أحدِ الطَّرفين يَستلزم امتناعَ الآخَرِ ، فَعدمُها يستلزم عدمَهُ . (قَوْلُهُ : بَلْ يَكُوْنُ الْحُكْمُ ... إلخ) يتراءى منهُ أنَّ في القضيَّة الممكنة حكماً بالإيجاب أو السَّلْبِ، وقد عرفتَ أنْ لَا حُكْمَ فيها ، فَلْيُحْمَلِ الحُكْمُ على الحكم الموهوم نظراً إلى ظاهر العبارة .ا.هـ. عصام، ولذلك قال مير أبو الفتح : إنَّ الممكنةً العامَّةَ قَضيَّةُ بالقوَّةِ لا بالفعل، وبيانُهُ أنَّا إذا قُلْنَا : الإنسانُ كاتبُ بِالإمكان العام؛ فليس الحُكْمُ فيها إلَّا بِسَلبِ الضَّرورةِ عن الجانب المخالفِ، وأمَّا الحُكمُ في الجانب الموافقِ؛ فَلَمْ يتعرَّض لهُ حتَّى يحتمل أن يكون واقعاً وأن لا يكون ا.هـ. وقال عبد الحكيم : إنَّ الممكنةَ مُشتملة على الحكم باعتبار الجهة؛ لا بحسب ذاتها . (قَوْلُهُ : إِذِ السَّلْبُ خِلافُ النِّسْبَةِ قالَ شارحُ الغَرَّةِ : الشَّيءُ إذا لم يكن مُخالفه ضروريا ; فَنفسُهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ ضروريا ; فحينئذ تصدقُ قضيَّةٌ ضروريَّةٌ مُوافقةٌ لِمَفهوم القضيَّةِ؛ لا ممكنةٌ خاصَّةٌ ; لأنَّ أحدَ الطَّرفين ضروري، وإِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ غيرَ ضروري، بل يجوز ارتفاعه، فتصدقُ ممكنة خاصة لعدم ضرورةِ الطَّرفين، فَهَذهِ القضيَّةُ قد تتحقَّقُ معَ الضَّروريّةِ، وقد تتحقَّقُ مع الممكنة دونَ الضَّروريّةِ.
Rather, the judgment is by the non-necessity of the opposite of the relation; (the general possible). As in our saying: 'Every fire is hot' with general possibility, so it is judged in it by the non-necessity of negation, since negation is the opposite of the relation. Al-Dasuqi said: (His saying: the general possible) know that general possibility is the negation of necessity from the opposite side, and specific possibility is the negation of necessity from the opposite side and the agreeing side. The agreeing side is what the proposition conveys of the relation, and the opposite side is the opposite of the relation. (His saying: hot) i.e., it is valid for it to be hot, and its validity is when the negation is not necessary. (His saying: the negation) i.e., the negation of heat. His saying: (and if not) it is complete, and its subject is the possibility of negation, and the two expressions are equivalent as in al-Sayyid. Al-‘Isam discussed this, that the negation of essential impossibility from the agreeing side, even if it entails the negation of essential necessity from the opposite side and vice versa, but they are not co-extensive unless equivalence is intended in terms of actualization, not in the common sense of truth in the relations of concepts. The equivalence here is in terms of actualization because the necessity of one side entails the impossibility of the other, so its negation entails its negation. (His saying: rather the judgment...) it appears from it that in the possible proposition there is a judgment of affirmation or negation, and you know that there is no judgment in it, so let the judgment be interpreted as an imaginary judgment considering the apparent wording. So said al-‘Isam. Therefore Mir Abu al-Fath said: The general possible proposition is a potential proposition, not actual. Its explanation: when we say 'Man is a writer' with general possibility, there is no judgment in it except the negation of necessity from the opposite side. As for the judgment on the agreeing side, it is not addressed, so it may be actual or not. And ‘Abd al-Hakim said: The possible proposition includes judgment in terms of modality, not in terms of its essence. (His saying: since negation is the opposite of the relation) The commentator of al-Ghurra said: If something's opposite is not necessary, then itself is either necessary—then a necessary proposition is true, agreeing with the concept of the proposition, not a specific possible, because one of the two sides is necessary—or it is not necessary, rather its removal is permissible, then a specific possible is true due to the non-necessity of the two sides. So this proposition can be realized with the necessary, and can be realized with the possible without the necessary.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage defines the general possible proposition (al-mumkina al-‘amma) and distinguishes it from the specific possible. The author first states that a general possible proposition is one where the judgment is the non-necessity of the opposite of the relation. An example is 'Every fire is hot' with general possibility; the judgment here is that the negation (that fire is not hot) is not necessary, because negation is the opposite of the affirmed relation. Al-Dasuqi then clarifies: general possibility is the negation of necessity from the opposite side only, while specific possibility is the negation of necessity from both sides (the agreeing and the opposite). The agreeing side is what the proposition states (the relation), the opposite side is its opposite. He then explains the example 'hot': it means it is valid for fire to be hot, and its validity is conditional on the negation not being necessary. The commentary then discusses the equivalence of two expressions: 'negation of essential impossibility from the agreeing side' and 'negation of essential necessity from the opposite side'. Al-‘Isam notes that these are equivalent only in terms of actualization, not in the common sense of truth, because necessity of one side implies impossibility of the other. The passage then addresses a doubt: it might seem that a possible proposition involves a judgment of affirmation or negation, but in reality there is no positive judgment; the judgment is merely the negation of necessity. Mir Abu al-Fath explains that a general possible proposition is potential, not actual; when we say 'Man is a writer' with general possibility, we only negate the necessity of the opposite (that man is necessarily not a writer), but we do not assert actual writing. Finally, the commentator of al-Ghurra elaborates: if a thing's opposite is not necessary, then the thing itself is either necessary—yielding a necessary proposition—or not necessary, yielding a specific possible proposition. Thus the general possible can coexist with the necessary or with the specific possible.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام
الإمكان الخاص
الطرف الموافق
الطرف المخالف
الممكنة العامة

Checkpoint
According to Al-Dasuqi, what is the definition of general possibility (الإمكان العام)?
A) Negation of necessity from both the agreeing and opposite sides.
B) Affirmation of possibility from the agreeing side.
C) Negation of necessity from the opposite side only.
D) Affirmation of necessity from the opposite side.

Key Takeaway
General possibility negates necessity from the opposite side only, while specific possibility negates necessity from both sides.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following proposition expresses general or specific possibility: 'The world is possibly created.' Use the definitions from the passage to justify your answer.
?
Construct an example from fiqh: a ruling that is permissible (mubah) but not obligatory. Explain why it is a case of general possibility, not specific possibility.

Propositions
advanced
10 min
Understanding the Judgment in Possible Propositions
Al-Dasuqi defines general possibility and distinguishes it from specific possibility with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a general possible proposition does not contain a positive judgment, and articulate the positions of al-‘Isam, Mir Abu al-Fath, and ‘Abd al-Hakim.

Core Idea
A possible proposition is a judgment of potentiality, not actuality.

The passage extends the definition of general possibility into a subtle debate: does a general possible proposition actually assert something positive? Al-Dasuqi's wording might imply a judgment of affirmation or negation, but later scholars like al-‘Isam and Mir Abu al-Fath clarify that the judgment is only the negation of necessity. The proposition 'Man is a writer' with general possibility asserts nothing about actual writing; it only negates that he is necessarily not a writer. This deepens our understanding of modal propositions and their role in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Al-Dasuqi says 'the judgment is by the non-necessity of the opposite'. This might suggest there is a judgment of negation. But he also says there is no judgment in a possible proposition. Find these statements in the text.
Look at the section after قوله بَلْ يَكُوْنُ الْحُكْمُ.
2
Understand al-‘Isam's solution
Al-‘Isam resolves the contradiction by saying the judgment is 'imaginary' (موهوم), i.e., only apparent from the wording, not real.
3
Examine Mir Abu al-Fath's account
Mir Abu al-Fath states that a general possible proposition is a proposition in potentiality (بالقوة), not in actuality (بالفعل). The only judgment is the negation of necessity from the opposite side. There is no judgment on the agreeing side.
Example: 'الإنسان كاتب بالإمكان العام' means we only negate the necessity of not-writing.
4
Relate to the equivalence discussion
Al-‘Isam also discussed the equivalence of two expressions: 'negation of essential impossibility from the agreeing side' and 'negation of essential necessity from the opposite side'. He says they are equivalent in actualization, not in the common sense of truth. This reinforces that the proposition does not assert anything positive.

(قَوْلُهُ : إِذِ السَّلْبُ خِلافُ النِّسْبَةِ قالَ شارحُ الغَرَّةِ : الشَّيءُ إذا لم يكن مُخالفه ضروريا ; فَنفسُهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ ضروريا ; فحينئذ تصدقُ قضيَّةٌ ضروريَّةٌ مُوافقةٌ لِمَفهوم القضيَّةِ؛ لا ممكنةٌ خاصَّةٌ ; لأنَّ أحدَ الطَّرفين ضروري، وإِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ غيرَ ضروري، بل يجوز ارتفاعه، فتصدقُ ممكنة خاصة لعدم ضرورةِ الطَّرفين، فَهَذهِ القضيَّةُ قد تتحقَّقُ معَ الضَّروريّةِ، وقد تتحقَّقُ مع الممكنة دونَ الضَّروريّةِ.

The General Possible: Conditions for Possibility
Author defines the general possible and its conditions using conditional logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for the general possible proposition and explain how they are derived through conditional reasoning.

Core Idea
Possibility means absence of necessity, not actual existence of alternatives.

In Islamic logic, modal propositions describe the mode in which a predicate holds for a subject. The 'general possible' (الممكنة العامة) is a fundamental modal proposition that affirms or denies with possibility, meaning the opposite is not necessary. In this lesson, we break down its two conditions—non-necessity of negation and non-necessity of affirmation—and see how the author uses conditional 'if-then' reasoning to establish them. Understanding this foundation is crucial for grasping more specific modal propositions like the 'special possible'.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the general possible as a modal proposition
The general possible is a categorical proposition (subject-predicate) with the modality 'with general possibility' (بالإمكان العام). It can be affirmative (e.g., 'Fire is hot with general possibility') or negative (e.g., 'Nothing hot is cold with general possibility'). The key is that the modality does not assert necessity or impossibility.
Modal propositions are the building blocks of syllogisms in Islamic logic.
2
Identify the condition for the affirmative general possible
For an affirmative proposition like 'Fire is hot with general possibility', the condition is 'non-necessity of negation' (عدم ضرورة السلب). This means that the negation (Fire is not hot) is not necessary. If negation were necessary, then affirmation would be impossible. The author writes: 'If there were no non-necessity of negation, then affirmation would not be possible.'
The condition is derived from a conditional: if the opposite were necessary, the proposition would be false.
3
Identify the condition for the negative general possible
For a negative proposition like 'Nothing hot is cold with general possibility', the condition is 'non-necessity of affirmation' (عدم ضرورة الإيجاب). This means that affirmation (Hot is cold) is not necessary. The author explains: 'If there were no non-necessity of affirmation, then negation would not be possible.'
Notice the symmetry: each form's condition is the non-necessity of its opposite.
4
Apply the conditional reasoning to examples
Take the negative example: 'لا شيء من الحارّ ببارد بالإمكان العام' (Nothing hot is cold with general possibility). The author says it is 'judged with non-necessity of affirmation' because affirmation would be contrary to the relation. This means that the affirmation (hot is cold) is not necessary, so negation is possible. The same reasoning works for affirmative examples.
The proposition can coincide with the necessary (if one side is necessary) or with the possible without necessity (if neither side is necessary).
5
Contrast with the special possible to solidify the concept
The author notes the general possible is called 'general' because it is more comprehensive than the 'special possible' (الممكنة الخاصة) which will be studied later in compound propositions. The general possible includes both cases where the opposite is merely possible and where the opposite is necessary? Actually the general possible only requires non-necessity, so it includes cases where one side is necessary? The passage says it may coincide with the necessary: 'قد تتحقَّقُ معَ الضَّروريّةِ' meaning the proposition can be true even when one side is necessary? Wait, careful: 'قد تتحقَّقُ معَ الضَّروريّةِ' likely means that the proposition can be stated alongside a necessary proposition (i.e., the same subject-predicate can be necessary in another case). But the general possible itself cannot be necessary because it asserts possibility. Actually the passage says: 'فهذه القضية قد تتحقق مع الضرورية وقد تتحقق مع الممكنة دون الضرورية' – meaning that the general possible proposition may exist alongside a necessary proposition (maybe as a different proposition), or alongside a possible proposition without the necessary. So the general possible does not prevent necessity from holding in a different context. This is a subtle point.
The general possible is defined solely by the absence of necessity of its opposite, not by any positive modal force.

فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
ولو لم يكن عدم ضرورةِ السَّلبِ ؛ لم يكن الإيجاب ممكناً، وكَقولِنَا : لا شيء من الحارّ ببارد بالإمكان العام، فحُكِمَ فيها بعدم ضرورة الإيجاب؛ إذ الإيجاب خلافُ النِّسبةِ،
فَهَذِهِ القضيَّةُ قد تتحقَّقُ معَ الضَّروريّةِ، وقد تتحقَّقُ مع الممكنة دونَ الضَّروريّةِ.
ولو لم يكن عدم ضرورة الإيجاب؛ لم يكن السَّلب ممكناً، فمعنى الموجبة : أنَّ سلب الحرارةِ عن النَّار ليس بضروريّ، ومعنى السالبة : أنَّ إيجاب البرودة للحار ليس بضروريّ.
وسمّيت ممكنةً ؛ لاشتمالها على معنى الإمكان، وعامَّةٌ ؛ لكونها أعمّ من الممكنة الخاصة التي ستعرفها في المركَّبات.
Chapter: On the Modal Proposition and Its Divisions. And if there were no non-necessity of negation, then affirmation would not be possible, such as our saying: 'Nothing hot is cold with general possibility', so it is judged therein by the non-necessity of affirmation, since affirmation is contrary to the relation. So this proposition may coincide with the necessary, and may coincide with the possible without the necessary. And if there were no non-necessity of affirmation, then negation would not be possible. So the meaning of the affirmative: that negation of heat from fire is not necessary; and the meaning of the negative: that affirmation of coldness for the hot is not necessary. And it is called 'possible' because it includes the meaning of possibility, and 'general' because it is more general than the special possible which you will learn of in the compound [propositions].
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage defines the general possible proposition (الممكنة العامة) and explains its conditions. The author begins by stating a conditional: if there were no 'non-necessity of negation', meaning if negation were necessary, then affirmation would be impossible. An example is given: 'Nothing hot is cold with general possibility' (لا شيء من الحارّ ببارد بالإمكان العام). In such a proposition, it is judged that affirmation is not necessary, because affirmation contradicts the relation (i.e., the relation between subject and predicate). The author then clarifies that this proposition can sometimes coincide with a necessary proposition (when one side is necessary) and sometimes with a possible proposition without necessity (when both sides are non-necessary). The same conditional reasoning is applied to the non-necessity of affirmation: if there were no non-necessity of affirmation, then negation would not be possible. Therefore, the meaning of the affirmative general possible is that the negation of heat from fire is not necessary, and the meaning of the negative general possible is that the affirmation of coldness for the hot is not necessary. Finally, the author explains why it is called 'possible' (ممكنة) – because it includes the meaning of possibility – and 'general' (عامة) – because it is more general than the 'special possible' (الممكنة الخاصة) which will be introduced later in the section on compound propositions. This passage thus establishes the foundation for understanding modal propositions in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
ممكنة عامة
عدم ضرورة السلب
عدم ضرورة الإيجاب

C) The affirmation is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The general possible proposition is defined by the absence of necessity of its opposite, making it the broadest modal proposition.

Simple and Compound Modal Propositions
Author classifies modal propositions into simple and compound types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between simple and compound modal propositions and identify which modal propositions are omitted from the simple list based on logical entailments.

Core Idea
Simple modals have one modality; absolute necessary modals are omitted because essential necessity entails all other modalities.

In this lesson, we examine the classification of modal propositions into simple (بسائط) and compound (مركبات) as presented by the author. The simple ones are those that contain only affirmation or negation, while the compounds number seven. Importantly, the author explains why some common modal propositions, like the absolute necessary, are not included among the simples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for simplicity
Notice that the author defines simple modal propositions as those whose meaning is either affirmation only or negation only. This means they have a single modality without combination.
The simplicity is about the number of modalities, not the complexity of the content.
2
Distinguish between simple and compound
List the compound modal propositions (seven in number). They combine modalities such as necessity and possibility, or perpetuity and contingency. Simple ones are the rest of the modal propositions that are not compounds.
The passage does not list the seven compounds; they are explained elsewhere.
3
Understand the omission of absolute necessary
The author says that the absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة) is dropped from the simples because it cannot accept further restriction. Essential necessity already entails descriptive necessity and absolute perpetuity, so adding 'by necessity' or 'by perpetuity' would be redundant or contradictory.
This is because the absolute necessary is already fully determined; any additional modality would conflict.
4
Note the omission of absolute perpetual
Similarly, the absolute perpetual (الدائمة المطلقة) is also dropped for analogous reasons: its perpetuity is absolute and cannot be further qualified.
This completes the list of omitted modal propositions from the simples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَهَذِهِ القضايا المذكورة بَسَائِطُ؛ لأنَّ معناها إما إيجاب فقط، أو سلب فقط. وأما المركَّباتُ فَسَبْعٌ، وسقط من البسائط الضَّروريَّةُ المطلقة؛ لأنَّها لا تقبلُ التَّقييد، فإنَّ الضَّرورةَ الذَّاتِيَّةَ تَستلزمُ الضَّرورة الوصفيَّةَ والدَّوامِ مُطلقاً، فَلَا تقبلُ التَّقييد لا باللأضرورةِ وَلَا بِاللأدوام لأنَّه تناقض، وسقط الدائمة المطلقة
So these mentioned propositions are simple, because their meaning is either affirmation only or negation only. And as for the compounds, they are seven. And the absolute necessary [propositions] are dropped from the simples because they do not accept restriction, for the essential necessity entails the descriptive necessity and absolute perpetuity, so they do not accept restriction neither by necessity nor by perpetuity because it is a contradiction. And the absolute perpetual is also dropped.

Commentary
This passage classifies modal propositions into simple (بسائط) and compound (مركبات). The simple ones are those that express only affirmation or only negation without combining modalities; their meaning is either simply affirmative or simply negative. The compound ones are seven in number, which are explained later (likely involving combination of necessity, perpetuity, etc.). The author then notes that some modal propositions are omitted from the simple list: the 'absolute necessary' (الضرورية المطلقة) – these are propositions that are necessarily true in an essential sense, such as 'Man is animal'. They are omitted because they cannot be further restricted (تقيد) by additional modalities; essential necessity already entails descriptive necessity and absolute perpetuity, so adding 'by necessity' or 'by perpetuity' would be redundant or contradictory. Similarly, the 'absolute perpetual' (الدائمة المطلقة) is dropped. This explains why the list of simples does not include these, even though they are valid modalities. The classification helps in understanding how modal propositions are built and which combinations are possible.

Understanding General Possibility in Modal Propositions
Author defines affirmative and negative modal propositions as instances of general possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'general possibility' (al-imkan al-'amm) and identify its affirmative and negative forms in Islamic logical analysis.

Core Idea
General possibility is the negation of necessity from the opposite side – the common-sense understanding of 'possible'.

This passage establishes the foundation for modal logic in the Islamic tradition by defining 'possible' propositions. The key insight is that possibility is defined negatively: it is the absence of necessity of the opposite. This definition is then applied to affirmative and negative propositions, which are both instances of general possibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Conditional Premise
The author begins with a conditional: if the absence of necessity of affirmation were not the case, then negation would be possible. Recognize that this sets the logical groundwork that possibility is defined against necessity.
The conditional shows that possibility requires that the opposite is not necessary.
2
Extract the Definition of the Affirmative Possible
The affirmative possible (al-mujiba) is defined as 'the negation of heat from fire is not necessary.' This means the proposition asserts the predicate but does not necessitate its opposite.
Example: 'Fire is hot' as a possible proposition means it is not necessary that fire is not hot.
3
Extract the Definition of the Negative Possible
The negative possible (al-saliba) is defined as 'the affirmation of coldness for the hot is not necessary.' This means the proposition denies the predicate but does not necessitate its affirmation.
Example: 'Hot is not cold' as a possible proposition means it is not necessary that hot is cold.
4
Recognize the Concept of General Possibility
Both affirmative and negative possible are called 'general possibility' (imkan 'amm) because they negate necessity from only one side. This is the common understanding of possibility among the general public: anything that is not impossible.
Al-Attar explains that 'general possibility' is what the general public use – it is simply 'not impossible'.
5
Distinguish from Specific Possibility
The passage notes that general possibility is more general than the 'specific possible' (imkan khas) to be introduced later. Understand that specific possibility will involve negation of necessity from both sides.
This distinction is foundational for compound modal propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو لم يكن عدم ضرورة الإيجاب؛ لم يكن السَّلب ممكناً، فمعنى الموجبة : أنَّ سلب الحرارةِ عن النَّار ليس بضروريّ، ومعنى السالبة : أنَّ إيجاب البرودة للحار ليس بضروريّ. وسمّيت ممكنةً ؛ لاشتمالها على معنى الإمكان، وعامَّةٌ ؛ لكونها أعمّ من الممكنة الخاصة التي ستعرفها في المركَّبات.
الدسوقي: قوله: ولو لم يكن ... إلخ أي : بل كان ضروريا، وقوله: (لم يكنِ السَّلْبُ)؛ أي: سَلْبُ البرودةِ عَن الحارّ ، وقوله : (مُمكناً)؛ أي بل مُستحيلاً ؛ أي : والفرضُ أنه ممكن.
العطار: قوله : لِاشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى مَعْنَى الْإِمْكَانِ وهو سَلْبُ الضَّرورةِ عَنِ الطَّرفِ المخالفِ.
العطار: قوله : لِاشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى مَعْنَى الْإِمْكَانِ اشتمالُ الكُلِّ على الجزء في المعقولة، والدَّالّ على المدلولِ في الملفوظة، قال شارح المطالع : إِنَّما سُمِّيَ إمكاناً عاماً ؛ لأنَّهُ المستعمل عند جمهور العامَّةِ، فإنَّهم يفهمونَ مِنَ الممكن؛ ما ليس بممتنع، ومِمَّا ليس بممكن ؛ الممتنع.
And if the absence of necessity of affirmation were not, then negation would not be possible. So the meaning of the affirmative is that the negation of heat from fire is not necessary, and the meaning of the negative is that the affirmation of coldness for the hot is not necessary. And it is called 'possible' because it includes the meaning of possibility, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific possible that you will learn about in the compounds. Al-Dasuqi: his saying: 'And if it were not...' etc., i.e., rather it was necessary, and his saying: 'negation would not be' meaning the negation of coldness from the hot, and his saying: 'possible' meaning rather impossible, i.e., and the assumption is that it is possible. Al-Attar: his saying: 'because it includes the meaning of possibility' and it is the negation of necessity from the opposite side. Al-Attar: his saying: 'because it includes the meaning of possibility' is the inclusion of the whole of the part in the intelligible, and the signifier of the signified in the uttered. The commentator on Al-Mutali' said: It was only called 'general possibility' because it is what the general public use, for they understand from the possible what is not impossible, and from what is not possible: the impossible.

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of simple modal propositions: affirmative (al-mujiba) and negative (al-saliba) as they apply to possibility. The author begins by stating a conditional: if the absence of necessity of the affirmative were not the case (i.e., if affirmation were necessary), then negation would not be possible. This sets the stage for defining each. The affirmative modal proposition (mumkina mujiba) means that negating the predicate from the subject is not necessary. For example, 'Fire is hot' as a possible proposition means that it is not necessary that fire be not hot (i.e., it is not necessarily cold). Similarly, the negative modal proposition (mumkina saliba) means that affirming the opposite predicate is not necessary. The author then explains why these are called 'possible'—because they include the meaning of possibility, i.e., negation of necessity from the opposite side. They are also called 'general possibility' because they are broader than the 'specific possible' that will appear later in compound modalities. Al-Dasuqi's commentary clarifies the conditional: if the absence of necessity were not the case, then negation would be impossible. Al-Attar then expands on the term 'inclusion of the meaning of possibility': possibility is defined as the negation of necessity from the contrary side. He also notes that general possibility is what common people mean by 'possible'—anything that is not impossible. The distinction between general and specific possibility is foundational for understanding compound modal propositions.

Key Takeaway
General possibility is the negation of necessity from the opposite side; it is the common understanding of possibility and underlies both affirmative and negative modal propositions in Islamic logic.

The Seven Compound Modal Propositions
Author classifies modal propositions into simples and compounds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which modal propositions are simple and which are compound, and explain why the absolute necessary and absolute perpetual propositions are excluded from the seven compounds.

Core Idea
Only propositions that can be consistently qualified become compound; absolute necessary and perpetual cannot.

In this passage, the author classifies modal propositions into simple and compound, emphasizing that there are seven compound types. The absolute necessary and absolute perpetual are dropped because they resist qualification without contradiction. Understanding this taxonomy is crucial for constructing valid modal syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of simple modal propositions
Simple propositions express only affirmation or negation without additional modal qualification. The author states that the previously mentioned propositions (possibility) are simple because their meaning is only affirmation or negation.
These are the basic possible propositions: affirmative and negative.
2
List the seven compound modal propositions
The author says compounds are seven. Although not listed here, from the context they are the qualified forms of the possible, necessary, perpetual, etc. Memorize that compounds are seven, not eight.
The absolute necessary and absolute perpetual are excluded from the list.
3
Explain why the absolute necessary is excluded
The absolute necessary proposition (e.g., 'Every human is necessarily rational') implies descriptive necessity and absolute perpetuity. Adding a qualification like 'non-necessary' or 'non-perpetual' would contradict its essential meaning.
Al-Dasuqi states: 'لا تقبل التقييد' because it leads to contradiction.
4
Explain why the absolute perpetual is excluded
Similarly, the absolute perpetual proposition (e.g., 'Fire is perpetually hot') cannot be qualified by 'non-perpetual' as that would deny its perpetuity. Also, it cannot be qualified by 'non-necessary' because, as later commentary argues, perpetuity always accompanies necessity.
The exclusion is due to logical impossibility of consistent qualification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَهَذِهِ القضايا المذكورة بسائط؛ لأنَّ معناها إما إيجاب فقط، أو سلب فقط. أقسام الموَجَّهَةِ المركَّبَة ] وأما المركَّباتُ فَسَبْعٌ،
الدسوقي: قوله: وأما المركبات فسبع لانه سقط من البسائط الضرورية المطلقة؛ لأنها لا تقبل التقييد، فإن الضرورة الذاتية تستلزم الضرورة الوصفية والدوام مطلقا، فلا تقبل التقييد لا باللاضرورة ولا باللادوام لأنه تناقض، وسقط الدائمة المطلقة.
العطار: قوله: بسائط لم يعرّفُهُ بـ «أل» ؛ لئلا يقتضي حصر البسائط في هذه، وليس كذلك ؛ بل هناك بسائط غيرُها سيأتي بعضُها في بابِ التَّناقض، ووقع في نسخة الجلالِ مُعرَّفاً بـ «أل» فقال : يعني المعتبرة. ا.هـ. فذكر القيد لتصحيح الحصرِ.
ثم العطار: فإنَّها لا تقبلُ التَّقييد باللادوام للتناقض، وأما عدم تقييدها باللاضرورة فتوقف فيه المحشّي، لأنَّ الشَّيء قد يكون دائماً ولا يكون ضروريا كَالسَّواد للزنجي، فما المانعُ مِن أنْ يُقالَ: كُلُّ زنجي أسودُ دائماً لا بالضَّرورة؛ انتهى، قلنا: المانع عدم الاضطرادِ إذْ قد يكونُ الشَّيءُ دائماً على جهةِ الضَّرورة، فهذا تقييد غيرُ معتبر، وسيأتي لهذا بقيَّةٌ على أنَّهُ نصَّ في شرح المطالعِ على أنَّهُ لا يكونُ الدَّوامُ إلا مع الوجوبِ، قال: وعلى هذا ؛ يتساوى الدَّوامُ والضَّرورةُ بحسبِ الصّدقِ .ا.هـ. وحينئذٍ لا تقبلُ العامَّةُ التَّقييد باللاضرورة، وقولُ المحشّي : إنَّ بعضَ القضايا يُقيَّدُ بِاللَّاضرورة وبعضها باللادوام؛ سهو، فَإِنَّهُ لا يقيَّدُ بِاللَّاضرورةِ إِلَّا الوجوديَّةُ، اللاضروريَّةُ، وَمَا عداهَا يُقيَّدُ بِاللادوام، لا يُقالُ: أراد الممكنة الخاصَّةَ أيضاً؛ لأنَّ فيها سَلْبَ الضَّرورةِ عَن الطَّرفين، قُلْنَا : هِيَ غيرُ مُقيَّدةٍ صريحاً وإنْ كانَ كلامُ المصنِّفِ الآتي يُشعر بذلك، على أنَّ المحشّي قالَ: وَلَا يظهرُ في الممكنة الخاصة .
So these mentioned propositions are 'simple' because their meaning is either solely affirmation or solely negation. The sections of the compound modal proposition: 'As for compounds, they are seven.'
Al-Dasuqi: his saying: 'As for compounds, they are seven' because from the simples the absolute necessary fell away, for they do not accept qualification, for essential necessity entails descriptive necessity and absolute perpetuity, so it does not accept qualification by non-necessity nor by non-perpetuity because that is a contradiction. Also the absolute perpetual fell away.
Al-Attar: his saying: 'simple' he did not define it with 'al' so as not to imply restriction of the simples to these, and it is not so, for there are other simples, some of which will come in the chapter on contradiction. And it occurred in the version of al-Jalal defined with 'al' and he said: meaning the considered ones. End. So he mentioned the condition to correct the restriction.
Then al-Attar: For they (the absolute necessary and perpetual) do not accept qualification by non-perpetuity due to contradiction. As for not qualifying them by non-necessity, there is hesitation from the commentator, because a thing may be perpetual but not necessary, like blackness for the Black man. So what prevents saying: 'Every Black man is perpetually black, not by necessity'? End. We say: the prevention is the lack of universality, for a thing may be perpetually in the manner of necessity. So this qualification is not considered. There will be a remainder for this, and he explicitly stated in the commentary on Al-Mutali' that perpetuity does not occur except with necessity. He said: upon this, perpetuity and necessity are equal with respect to truth. End. Then the general (possibility) does not accept qualification by non-necessity. And the commentator's saying that some propositions are qualified by non-necessity and some by non-perpetuity is an oversight, for only the existential non-necessary is qualified by non-necessity, and what is other than it is qualified by non-perpetuity. It cannot be said: He also intended the specific possible, because it involves negation of necessity from both sides. We say: it is not explicitly qualified, even though the author's upcoming speech suggests that, and the commentator said: it does not appear in the specific possible.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage classifies modal propositions into 'simple' (basait) and 'compound' (murakkabat) and explains why the number of compounds is seven rather than eight. The author first reiterates that the propositions previously defined (affirmative and negative possibility) are simple because their meaning consists only of affirmation or negation. Then he introduces the compound propositions: there are seven. Al-Dasuqi explains why: from the original eight simples (which include absolute necessary, absolute perpetual, etc.), two are dropped: the absolute necessary and the absolute perpetual. These cannot be qualified because essential necessity implies descriptive necessity and absolute perpetuity, so adding qualifications like 'not necessary' or 'not perpetual' would be contradictory. Similarly, absolute perpetual cannot be qualified by 'not perpetual' because it is by definition perpetual. Al-Attar first comments on why 'simple' is not defined with the definite article: to avoid implying that only these are simple; other simples exist (like those appearing in the chapter on contradiction). Then he addresses a subtle point: some might think absolute perpetual can be qualified by 'not necessary' (e.g., 'Every Black man is perpetually black, but not necessarily'), but this is rejected because such a statement is not universal (it is coincidental). Moreover, in the commentary on Al-Mutali', perpetuity always accompanies necessity, so they are equivalent in truth. Therefore, general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) also cannot be qualified by non-necessity. The commentator's view that some propositions are qualified by non-necessity and some by non-perpetuity is mistaken; only existential non-necessary propositions take non-necessity, and others take non-perpetuity. The specific possible (al-imkan al-khas) is not explicitly qualified, though the author's later speech may suggest otherwise.

Key Terms
البسائط
المركبات
الضرورية المطلقة
الدائمة المطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are the absolute necessary and absolute perpetual propositions excluded from the seven compounds?
A) Because they are not considered modal propositions.
B) Because they do not accept qualification due to contradiction.
C) Because they are not real propositions.
D) Because they are self-contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary and absolute perpetual propositions cannot be compounded because any qualification would contradict their essential meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why Perpetual Propositions Cannot Be Qualified as Non-Necessary
Al-Attar discusses qualifying perpetual propositions by non-necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the argument that perpetual propositions cannot be qualified as 'not necessary', and identify the exceptions.

Core Idea
Perpetuity and necessity are equated in truth, making 'non-necessary' qualification impossible for perpetual propositions.

Al-Attar presents a counterexample: a Black man is perpetually black but not necessarily black. However, he argues this lacks universality. The commentary on Al-Mutali' states that perpetuity always accompanies necessity, so any perpetual proposition is also necessary. Therefore, qualifying it as 'not necessary' is contradictory. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and clarifies the correct principles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the commentator's hesitation
The commentator (al-Muhashshi) suggests that some perpetual propositions might be qualified as 'not necessary', giving the example of blackness in a Black man. Note that this example appears to show a perpetual property that is not essential.
This illustrates a potential exception to the rule.
2
Analyze Al-Attar's refutation
Al-Attar rejects the example because it lacks universality (al-istitrad). The proposition 'Every Black man is perpetually black' is not universally true in a necessary sense; it is a coincidence. Moreover, if something is perpetual in a non-necessary way, it could still be necessary in another aspect.
Al-Attar invokes the principle from Al-Mutali'.
3
Apply the principle from Al-Mutali'
The commentary on Al-Mutali' states that perpetuity never occurs without necessity (wujub). Therefore, every perpetual proposition is also necessary, making them equivalent in truth. Consequently, qualifying a perpetual proposition as 'not necessary' would be contradictory.
This principle solidifies the exclusion.
4
Identify the correct qualifying rules
The passage concludes that only existential non-necessary propositions can be qualified by 'non-necessity', while all others (including perpetual ones) are qualified by 'non-perpetuity'. General possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) also cannot be qualified by non-necessity because it already negates necessity on one side.
This clarifies the taxonomy of qualifications.

So these mentioned propositions are 'simple' because their meaning is either solely affirmation or solely negation. The sections of the compound modal proposition: 'As for compounds, they are seven.'
Al-Dasuqi: his saying: 'As for compounds, they are seven' because from the simples the absolute necessary fell away, for they do not accept qualification, for essential necessity entails descriptive necessity and absolute perpetuity, so it does not accept qualification by non-necessity nor by non-perpetuity because that is a contradiction. Also the absolute perpetual fell away.
Al-Attar: his saying: 'simple' he did not define it with 'al' so as not to imply restriction of the simples to these, and it is not so, for there are other simples, some of which will come in the chapter on contradiction. And it occurred in the version of al-Jalal defined with 'al' and he said: meaning the considered ones. End. So he mentioned the condition to correct the restriction.
Then al-Attar: For they (the absolute necessary and perpetual) do not accept qualification by non-perpetuity due to contradiction. As for not qualifying them by non-necessity, there is hesitation from the commentator, because a thing may be perpetual but not necessary, like blackness for the Black man. So what prevents saying: 'Every Black man is perpetually black, not by necessity'? End. We say: the prevention is the lack of universality, for a thing may be perpetually in the manner of necessity. So this qualification is not considered. There will be a remainder for this, and he explicitly stated in the commentary on Al-Mutali' that perpetuity does not occur except with necessity. He said: upon this, perpetuity and necessity are equal with respect to truth. End. Then the general (possibility) does not accept qualification by non-necessity. And the commentator's saying that some propositions are qualified by non-necessity and some by non-perpetuity is an oversight, for only the existential non-necessary is qualified by non-necessity, and what is other than it is qualified by non-perpetuity. It cannot be said: He also intended the specific possible, because it involves negation of necessity from both sides. We say: it is not explicitly qualified, even though the author's upcoming speech suggests that, and the commentator said: it does not appear in the specific possible.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage classifies modal propositions into 'simple' (basait) and 'compound' (murakkabat) and explains why the number of compounds is seven rather than eight. The author first reiterates that the propositions previously defined (affirmative and negative possibility) are simple because their meaning consists only of affirmation or negation. Then he introduces the compound propositions: there are seven. Al-Dasuqi explains why: from the original eight simples (which include absolute necessary, absolute perpetual, etc.), two are dropped: the absolute necessary and the absolute perpetual. These cannot be qualified because essential necessity implies descriptive necessity and absolute perpetuity, so adding qualifications like 'not necessary' or 'not perpetual' would be contradictory. Similarly, absolute perpetual cannot be qualified by 'not perpetual' because it is by definition perpetual. Al-Attar first comments on why 'simple' is not defined with the definite article: to avoid implying that only these are simple; other simples exist (like those appearing in the chapter on contradiction). Then he addresses a subtle point: some might think absolute perpetual can be qualified by 'not necessary' (e.g., 'Every Black man is perpetually black, but not necessarily'), but this is rejected because such a statement is not universal (it is coincidental). Moreover, in the commentary on Al-Mutali', perpetuity always accompanies necessity, so they are equivalent in truth. Therefore, general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) also cannot be qualified by non-necessity. The commentator's view that some propositions are qualified by non-necessity and some by non-perpetuity is mistaken; only existential non-necessary propositions take non-necessity, and others take non-perpetuity. The specific possible (al-imkan al-khas) is not explicitly qualified, though the author's later speech may suggest otherwise.

B) Necessity is more general than perpetuity.
C) They are equal in truth, and perpetuity never occurs without necessity.

Key Takeaway
Perpetual propositions cannot be qualified as 'not necessary' because perpetuity implies necessity, making such qualification contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the principles from Al-Attar and Al-Mutali', explain why the proposition 'Fire is perpetually hot' cannot be qualified as 'not necessary'. Provide a step-by-step reasoning.
?
Consider the proposition 'Some humans are perpetually black'. Can it be qualified as 'not necessary'? Why or why not? Refer to the concept of universality and the equivalence of perpetuity and necessity.
