Why Only Six Simple Propositions Yield Seven Compound Propositions
Commentators explain why only six simples accept modal qualifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which two simple propositions resist modal qualification and explain the logical reason for their resistance.

Core Idea
Necessary and perpetual by essence contradict their own negation — they cannot be modally qualified.

You know there are eight types of simple modal propositions (basā’iṭ) and seven types of compound propositions (murakkabāt). But that seems contradictory — if compounds are made from simples, why are there fewer? The commentators resolve this by showing that two of the simples — the absolute necessary and the absolute perpetual — cannot be qualified at all. Only six simples are actually eligible for modal qualification, and from these six, seven compounds arise because some simples can be qualified in two ways. This lesson walks you through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eight simples and seven compounds
Locate in the passage where the eight simples and seven compounds are mentioned. Note that the apparent contradiction is the starting point: eight simples should yield eight compounds if all were qualified, but there are only seven.
The compounds are the qualified versions of the simples.
2
Find the two exceptional simples
According to the commentators, the absolute necessary (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa) and the absolute perpetual (al-dā’ima al-muṭlaqa) are the two that cannot be qualified. The passage states they are 'not qualified at all' (lā yuqayyadāni aṣlan).
3
Understand why they resist qualification
Necessity by essence (al-ḍarūra al-dhātiyya) entails subjective perpetuity (al-dawām al-dhātī). So if you qualify the necessary with 'not necessary' or 'not perpetual', you deny its essential attribute — a contradiction. Similarly, the perpetual by essence cannot be qualified with 'not perpetual'.
The passage uses the examples: 'if the first were qualified with lack of necessity or lack of subjective perpetuity, it would be a contradiction' (kāna tanāquḍan).
4
Confirm the remaining six produce seven compounds
The passage says that among the six remaining, some can be qualified with both lack of perpetuity and lack of necessity (e.g., the temporal), while others take only one qualification. This yields seven distinct compound propositions.
Al-Dasuqi: 'one of them is qualified with lack of perpetuity and lack of necessity, and other than them is only qualified with one of them; therefore the compounds are seven.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعْرِفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ وهي بعينها البسائط المذكورة، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَهِيَ بِعَيْنِهَا ... إلخ) فيه أنَّ البسائط ثمانية، والمركباتِ سبعة، ففي الكلام تنافٍ؛ إلا أنْ يُقالَ : إِنَّ قولَهُ : :((بعينها) بمعنى : أَنَّ المركَّبَاتِ لا تخرجُ عَنِ البسائطِ، وإنْ كانَتْ أقل منها . ا . هـ . ياسين نقلاً . البسائطُ على حذفِ مُضاف؛ أي: هي بعضُ البسائط، أو يُقالُ : قوله : هي وَذلك لأنَّ الضَّروريّة المطلقة والدائمة المطلقة لا يُقيَّدانِ أصلاً؛ لأنَّ الضَّرورة في الأولى بحسب الذَّات وَهِيَ تَستلزمُ الدَّوامَ الذَّاتي، فَلَوْ قُيِّدَت الأُوْلَى بِاللَّاضرورةِ أو اللادوامِ الذَّاتي؛ كان تناقضاً، والدوام في الثَّانية بحسبِ الذَّاتِ، فَلَو قُيِّدَت باللادوام الذَّاتي ؛ كانَ تناقضاً، فَالباقي مِنَ البسائط سنَّةٌ، وَهِيَ الَّتي تُقيَّد، لكنَّ العطار فإنَّها لا تقبلُ التَّقييد باللادوام للتناقض، وأما عدم تقييدها باللاضرورة فتوقف فيه المحشّي، لأنَّ الشَّيء قد يكون دائماً ولا يكون ضروريا كَالسَّواد للزنجي، فما المانعُ مِن أنْ يُقالَ: كُلُّ زنجي أسودُ دائماً لا بالضَّرورة؛ انتهى، قلنا: المانع عدم الاضطرادِ إذْ قد يكونُ الشَّيءُ دائماً على جهةِ الضَّرورة، فهذا تقييد غيرُ معتبر، وسيأتي لهذا بقيَّةٌ على أنَّهُ نصَّ في شرح المطالعِ على أنَّهُ لا يكونُ الدَّوامُ إلا مع الوجوبِ، قال: وعلى هذا ؛ يتساوى الدَّوامُ والضَّرورةُ بحسبِ الصّدقِ .ا.هـ. وحينئذٍ لا تقبلُ العامَّةُ التَّقييد باللاضرورة، وقولُ المحشّي : إنَّ بعضَ القضايا يُقيَّدُ بِاللَّاضرورة وبعضها باللادوام؛ سهو، فَإِنَّهُ لا يقيَّدُ بِاللَّاضرورةِ إِلَّا الوجوديَّةُ، اللاضروريَّةُ، وَمَا عداهَا يُقيَّدُ بِاللادوام، لا يُقالُ: أراد الممكنة الخاصَّةَ أيضاً؛ لأنَّ فيها سَلْبَ الضَّرورةِ عَن الطَّرفين، قُلْنَا : هِيَ غيرُ مُقيَّدةٍ صريحاً وإنْ كانَ كلامُ المصنِّفِ الآتي يُشعر بذلك، على أنَّ المحشّي قالَ: وَلَا يظهرُ في الممكنة الخاصة . (قَوْلُهُ : وَهِيَ بِعَيْنِهَا الْبَسَائِطُ) أي : البسائط القابلة للتقييد؛ لَا كُلُّهَا؛ لِمَا علمت الضرورية، وأيضاً الممكنة العامة غيرُ مُقيَّدةٍ صريحاً باللاضرورة. من خروج لكن مع تقييدِهَا باللأدوام الذاتي، أو اللاضرورةِ الذاتية كما قال: (وَقَدْ تُقَيَّدُ) المشروطة والعرفيَّةُ (العَامَّتَانِ، وَ تُقَيَّدُ الوَقْتِيَتَانِ)؛ أي: الوقتيّة الدسوقي واحدة منها تُقيَّدُ بِاللادوامِ، وَبِاللاضرورةِ، وَغيرُهَا إِنَّمَا يُقيَّدُ بواحدٍ منهما؛ فَلِذَا كانَتِ المركَّبات سبعاً . إنْ قُلْتَ: عدمُ تَقييدِ الدَّائمةِ المطلقة باللا دوام ظاهرٌ مِمَّا قُلْنَا، وَلِمَ لَمْ تُقيَّدْ باللاضرورة؟؛ لأنَّه قد مر أنَّ الشَّيء قد يكون دائماً، وَلَا يكون ضروريا كـ: (السَّواد لِلزِّنجي)، فَمَا المانعُ مِنْ أنْ يُقالَ: دائماً كلُّ زنجي أسودُ لَا بِالضَّرورة؟! كَذَا بحث ياسين. وأجابَ شيخُنَا السَّيِّدُ البليديُّ : بأنَّ المانع من ذلك عدم الاطرادِ، تأمل. (قَوْلُهُ: بِاللَّا دَوَامِ) أي: بعدم الدَّوامِ الَّذي بحسب الذَّاتِ، وإِنَّما فُيِّدَتْ تلك الأربعةُ باللادوامِ الذاتي، ولم تُقيَّد باللاضرورة؛ لأنَّ اللادوامَ أخصُّ مِنَ اللاضرورةِ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ مِن عدم الدَّوامِ ؛ عدمُ الضَّرورة، من غير عكس؛ لاحتمال أن يكون شيء دائماً غير ضروري . انتهى تقرير، تأمل. العطار (قَوْلُهُ: بِاللَّادَوَامِ الذَّاتِي أَوِ الْلَّا ضَرَوْرَةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ) ينبغي أن يعلم أنَّ الضَّرورةَ خمس ؛ الأزليَّةُ : و وهي الحاصلة أزلاً وأبداً كَقولنا : الله تعالى عَالِمُ بالضَّرورةِ الأزليّةِ، والذَّاتِيَّةُ ؛ أي: الحاصلة ما دامَ ذاتُ الموضوع موجوداً، والوصفية : وهي الضرورة باعتبار وصف الموضوع، والضَّرورة بحسب وقتِ إِمَّا مُعيَّن أو غيرِ مُعيَّن، والضَّرورة بشرط المحمول : وهي ضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع سليه عنه بشرط ثبوتِ المحمول أو سلبه، وأنَّ الدَّوامَ ثلاثة أقسام ؛ الدَّوامُ الأزلي : وهو أن يكون المحمول ثابتاً للموضوع أو مسلوباً عنه أزلاً وأبداً كَقولنا : فلك متحرك بالدَّوامِ الأزلي، والدَّوامُ الذَّاتي : وهو أن يكون المحمول ثابتاً أو مسلوباً ما دامَ ذاتُ الموضوع موجوداً ، والدَّوامُ الوصفيُّ: وَهوَ أنْ يكونَ النُّبوتُ أو السَّلْبُ ما دامَ ذاتُ الموضوع موصوفاً بالوصف العنواني، أفاده في شرح المطالع، إذا علمت هذا ؛ فتقييدُ الضَّرورة المنفيَّةِ بالذَّاتِيَّةِ لِلاحترازِ عَمَّا عداهَا
Section: On al-ma‘rif and its divisions. And they are exactly the aforementioned simples. Al-Dasuqi (his statement: 'and they are exactly... etc.') contains that the simples are eight, and the compounds are seven, so in the speech there is contradiction; unless it is said: indeed his statement 'exactly' means that the compounds do not exit from the simples, even though they are fewer than them. End. Transmitted by Yasin. The simples (are) with an omitted genitive; i.e., they are some of the simples. Or it is said: his statement 'they are' is because the absolute necessary and the absolute perpetual are not qualified at all; because necessity in the first is according to essence, and it entails subjective perpetuity, so if the first were qualified with lack of necessity or lack of subjective perpetuity, it would be a contradiction. And perpetuity in the second is according to essence, so if it were qualified with lack of subjective perpetuity, it would be a contradiction. So the remaining from the simples are six, and they are the ones that are qualified. But al-‘Attar: indeed they do not accept qualification with lack of perpetuity due to contradiction. As for the lack of their qualification with lack of necessity, the commentator (al-muḥashshī) hesitated over it, because a thing may be perpetual and not necessary, like blackness for the Zanjī. So what prevents saying: 'Every Zanjī is black perpetually, not necessarily'? End. We said: the prevention is the lack of uniformity, since a thing may be perpetual in a necessary manner, so this qualification is not considered. And there will come a remainder of this, in that he stated in Sharḥ al-Maṭāli‘ that perpetuity is only with obligation. He said: and upon this, perpetuity and necessity are equivalent in terms of truth. End. Therefore, the general propositions do not accept qualification with lack of necessity. And the statement of the commentator that some propositions are qualified with lack of necessity and some with lack of perpetuity is an oversight. For only the existential [propositions] that are non-necessary are qualified with lack of necessity, and what other than them is qualified with lack of perpetuity. It is not said: he meant the particular possible as well; because in it there is negation of necessity from both sides. We said: it is not explicitly qualified, even if the speech of the author below suggests that. Moreover, the commentator said: 'And it does not appear in the particular possible.' (His statement: 'And they are exactly the simples') i.e., the simples capable of qualification, not all of them; because of what you know of the necessary, and also the general possible is not explicitly qualified with lack of necessity. But with their qualification with subjective lack of perpetuity or subjective lack of necessity, as he said: ('And they may be qualified') the conditional and conventional general, and the temporal two are qualified; i.e., the temporal. Al-Dasuqi: one of them is qualified with lack of perpetuity and lack of necessity, and other than them is only qualified with one of them; therefore the compounds are seven. If you say: the lack of qualification of the absolute perpetual with lack of perpetuity is apparent from what we said, but why was it not qualified with lack of necessity? Because it has passed that a thing may be perpetual and not necessary, like the blackness for the Zanjī. So what prevents saying: 'perpetually every Zanjī is black, not necessarily'? Such is the research of Yasin. And our master al-Sayyid al-Balīdī answered: the prevention is the lack of uniformity. Reflect. (His statement: 'with lack of perpetuity') i.e., with the absence of perpetuity which is according to essence. And those four were only qualified with subjective lack of perpetuity, and not qualified with lack of necessity; because lack of perpetuity is more specific than lack of necessity; because from absence of perpetuity, absence of necessity follows, but not conversely, due to the possibility of a thing being perpetual and not necessary. End of report. Reflect. Al-‘Attār (His statement: 'with subjective lack of perpetuity or subjective lack of necessity') It should be known that necessity is of five types: the eternal necessity, which is obtained from eternity to eternity, like our saying: Allah is knowing with eternal necessity; the subjective, i.e., obtained as long as the subject's essence exists; the attributive, which is necessity in consideration of the subject's attribute; the temporal necessity, either determinate or indeterminate; and the necessity on condition of the predicate, which is the necessity of affirming the predicate to the subject or negating it from it on condition of affirming or negating the predicate. And perpetuity has three divisions: eternal perpetuity, which is that the predicate is affirmed to the subject or negated from it from eternity to eternity, like our saying: the sphere is moving with eternal perpetuity; subjective perpetuity, which is that the predicate is affirmed or negated as long as the subject's essence exists; and attributive perpetuity, which is that the affirmation or negation is as long as the subject's essence is described with the titular description. This is conveyed in Sharḥ al-Maṭāli‘. If you know this, then the qualification of the negated necessity with subjective [necessity] is to exclude what is other than it.
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hāshiya) by al-Dasūqī and al-‘Aṭṭār on a text that classifies modal propositions and explains which simple propositions (basā’iṭ) can be qualified to form compound propositions (murakkabāt). The discussion arises from an apparent contradiction: there are eight simple propositions but only seven compound propositions. The commentators reconcile this by explaining that two of the simples—the absolute necessary (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa) and the absolute perpetual (al-dā’ima al-muṭlaqa)—cannot be qualified at all. The reason is that necessity by essence entails subjective perpetuity, so qualifying the necessary with lack of necessity or lack of perpetual would be self-contradictory. Similarly, the perpetual by essence cannot be qualified with lack of subjective perpetuity. Thus only six simples remain, and these are the ones that undergo qualification to produce seven compounds. The discussion then addresses a specific objection: why can the absolute perpetual not be qualified with lack of necessity, given that some things are perpetual but not necessary (e.g., a Zanjī being black perpetually but not necessarily)? The answer is that this qualification lacks uniformity (iṭṭirād), because sometimes perpetuity does coincide with necessity, and the definition of the absolute perpetual proposition does not allow for such exceptions. The commentators also clarify the types of necessity and perpetuity: necessity is divided into five kinds (eternal, subjective, attributive, temporal, and conditional), and perpetuity into three (eternal, subjective, and attributive). The qualification of the compound propositions is specifically with subjective perpetuity or necessity, not other types. This careful delineation ensures that the modal system remains logically consistent.

الضرورة الذاتية

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary and absolute perpetual propositions resist modal qualification because their defining attributes (necessity by essence and perpetuity by essence) would be contradicted by any negation of those attributes.

Propositions

10 min
Restrictions of Modal Propositions by al-Dawam and al-Darura
Author explains why only two of four modal types are restricted by both la-dawam and la-darura.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the four modal proposition types and determine which can be restricted by essential non-permanence (la-dawam dhati) and which by essential non-necessity (la-darura dhatiyya).

Core Idea
La-dawam is more specific than la-darura; not every perpetual proposition is necessary.

In this lesson, we explore how modal propositions are restricted by essential non-permanence and essential non-necessity. The commentator explains why only certain proposition types accept both restrictions, while others accept only one or none. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing compound modal propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four modal propositions discussed
The passage mentions: absolute perpetual (da'ima mutlaqa), universal conditional (mashruta 'amma), conventional ('urfiyya), and temporal (waqtiyya). Note that the absolute perpetual asserts uninterrupted permanence.
2
Determine which accept restriction by la-dawam and la-darura
According to the text, the universal conditional and the temporal propositions can be restricted by both la-dawam and la-darura. The absolute perpetual cannot be restricted by la-dawam (contradiction) but can be restricted by la-darura. The conventional proposition is restricted by only one of them.
The absolute perpetual already implies permanence, so adding 'non-permanent' is impossible.
3
Explain why la-dawam is more specific than la-darura
La-dawam (absence of permanence) entails la-darura (absence of necessity), but not vice versa. The example given: 'Every Zanjī is black' is perpetual (always true as long as Zanjī exists) but not necessary (it could be otherwise). Thus, if something is non-permanent, it must be non-necessary, but something can be non-necessary yet still perpetual.
4
Apply the classification to form the seven compound propositions
By combining each of the four base propositions with la-dawam or la-darura according to the allowed restrictions, we obtain seven distinct compound modal propositions. For instance, the universal conditional restricted by la-dawam is called the 'particular conditional' (mashruta khassa).
The number seven is derived from the permissible combinations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن مع تقييدِهَا باللأدوام الذاتي، أو اللاضرورةِ الذاتية كما قال: (وَقَدْ تُقَيَّدُ) المشروطة والعرفيَّةُ (العَامَّتَانِ، وَ تُقَيَّدُ الوَقْتِيَتَانِ)؛ أي: الوقتيّة واحِدَةٌ منها تُقيَّدُ بِاللادوامِ، وَبِاللاضرورةِ، وَغيرُهَا إِنَّمَا يُقيَّدُ بواحدٍ منهما؛ فَلِذَا كانَتِ المركَّبات سبعاً . إنْ قُلْتَ: عدمُ تَقييدِ الدَّائمةِ المطلقة باللا دوام ظاهرٌ مِمَّا قُلْنَا، وَلِمَ لَمْ تُقيَّدْ باللاضرورة؟؛ لأنَّه قد مر أنَّ الشَّيء قد يكون دائماً، وَلَا يكون ضروريا كـ: (السَّواد لِلزِّنجي)، فَمَا المانعُ مِنْ أنْ يُقالَ: دائماً كلُّ زنجي أسودُ لَا بِالضَّرورة؟! كَذَا بحث ياسين. وأجابَ شيخُنَا السَّيِّدُ البليديُّ : بأنَّ المانع من ذلك عدم الاطرادِ، تأمل. (قَوْلُهُ: بِاللَّا دَوَامِ) أي: بعدم الدَّوامِ الَّذي بحسب الذَّاتِ، وإِنَّما فُيِّدَتْ تلك الأربعةُ باللادوامِ الذاتي، ولم تُقيَّد باللاضرورة؛ لأنَّ اللادوامَ أخصُّ مِنَ اللاضرورةِ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ مِن عدم الدَّوامِ ؛ عدمُ الضَّرورة، من غير عكس؛ لاحتمال أن يكون شيء دائماً غير ضروري . انتهى تقرير، تأمل. (قَوْلُهُ: بِاللَّادَوَامِ الذَّاتِي أَوِ الْلَّا ضَرَوْرَةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ) ينبغي أن يعلم أنَّ الضَّرورةَ خمس؛ الأزليَّةُ : وهي الحاصلة أزلاً وأبداً كَقولنا : الله تعالى عَالِمُ بالضَّرورةِ الأزليّةِ، والذَّاتِيَّةُ : أي: الحاصلة ما دامَ ذاتُ الموضوع موجوداً، والوصفية : وهي الضرورة باعتبار وصف الموضوع، والضَّرورة بحسب وقتِ إِمَّا مُعيَّن أو غير مُعيَّن، والضَّرورة بشرط المحمول : وهي ضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه بشرط ثبوتِ المحمول أو سلبه، وأنَّ الدَّوامَ ثلاثة أقسام؛ الدَّوامُ الأزلي : وهو أن يكون المحمول ثابتاً للموضوع أو مسلوباً عنه أزلاً وأبداً كَقولنا : فلك متحرك بالدَّوامِ الأزلي، والدَّوامُ الذَّاتي : وهو أن يكون المحمول ثابتاً أو مسلوباً ما دامَ ذاتُ الموضوع موجوداً، والدَّوامُ الوصفيُّ: وَهوَ أنْ يكونَ الثُّبوتُ أو السَّلْبُ ما دامَ ذاتُ الموضوع موصوفاً بالوصف العنواني، أفاده في شرح المطالع، إذا علمت هذا؛ فتقييدُ الضَّرورة المنفيَّةِ بالذَّاتِيَّةِ لِلاحترازِ عَمَّا عداهَا، وَالمنتشرة المطلقتان بِاللَّادَوَام الذَّاتِي؛ أي: قد تُقيَّدُ كلُّ واحدة من هذه القضايا المذكورة باللادوامِ الذاتي (فَتُسَمَّى) المشروطة العامة المقيَّدة باللادوام؛ (المشرُوطَةَ الخَاصَّةَ) منصوب على أنَّه مفعولُ تُسمَّى». (قَوْلُهُ : الْمَشْرُوطَةَ الْخَاصَّةَ) ظاهرُ المصنِّفِ أنَّ المشروطة الخاصة هي المشروطة العامة بالمعنى الأَوَّلِ؛ مع قيدِ الدَّوامِ الذَّاتي. وقال بعضُهم : إنَّها بالمعنى الثَّاني تُقيَّدُ باللادوام أيضاً؛ أي: في نحو: «كلُّ مُنخسفٍ مُظلمٌ مَا دامَ مُنخسفاً لا دائماً»، لا في نحو : كلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان لا دائماً إن شاء الله . وَكَذَا الدَّوامُ، ولكنَّ الشَّارِحَ خَصَّصَ المحترز عنهُ بالضَّرورةِ الوصفيَّةِ والدَّوامِ الوصفي، ولعلَّ ذلك باعتبار أنَّ المعتبر في الضَّرورةِ والدَّوامِ؛ إنَّما هو الذَّاتي والوصفي دون البقيَّةِ؛ تَأَمَّلْ، يدلُّ لِذلكَ أَنَّ الضَّرورةَ الذَّاتِيَّةَ تقبلُ التَّقييد باللأضرورةِ الأزليَّةِ واللادوام الأزلي نحو: كُلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان بالضَّرورة؛ لَا بالضّرورةِ الأزليَّةِ وَلَا بالدوام الأزليّ، فهذهِ مُركَّبةٌ صحيحة، لكنَّهَا غيرُ مُعتبرةٍ، وإلا؛ لَزادَت المركَّباتُ كثيراً باعتبارِ قبولِ التَّقييدِ. (قَوْلُهُ : فَتُسَمَّى الْمَشْرُوْطَةُ الْعَامَّةُ الْمُقَيَّدَةُ بِالْادَوَام) نظرَ فِيهِ العصامُ بأَنَّ المشروطة العامَّةَ هِيَ المكيَّفةُ بالكيفيَّةِ الواحدة فقط؛ لا المكيفة بالكيفيتَيْنِ، فالمراد : ما هو مشروطةٌ عامَّةٌ قبلَ التَّقييدِ بِاللادوامِ، وَقِش عليه نظائره ا.هـ. وإِنَّما اعتبر في مفهوم المشروطةِ الخاصَّةِ تَقييدُ الحُكْمِ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي؛ لأَنَّهُ المعتبر في . (قَوْلُهُ : أيضاً ... إلخ) أي : كما تقيد بالدوام الذاتي، وفيه أن هذا الكلام وما قبله بعيد، بل لا معنى له فإن المصنف يريد تسمية المشروطة الخاصة بالمشروطة العامة؛ أي: قبل التقييد باللادوام لا بعده، وكذا يقال في البقية، وقد وجدتُ في عبارة العصام بعد إبداء هذه الملاحظة ما يؤيدني في هذا المراد فحمدت الله . ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي .
But with their restriction by essential non-permanence, or essential non-necessity as he said: 'And the two universal conditional (mashruta) and conventional ('urfiyya) may be restricted, and the two temporal (waqtiyya) are restricted'; meaning: one of the temporal ones is restricted by non-permanence and by non-necessity, and the others are only restricted by one of them; therefore the compounds are seven. If you say: The lack of restriction of the absolute perpetual (da'ima mutlaqa) by non-permanence is apparent from what we said, and why was it not restricted by non-necessity? Because it has passed that a thing may be perpetual but not necessary, like 'blackness for the Zanjī'. So what prevents one from saying: perpetually every Zanjī is black, not by necessity?! Such is the research of Yasīn. And our master al-Sayyid al-Balīdī answered: The prevention of that is lack of regularity, ponder. (His words: by non-permanence) i.e. by absence of permanence according to essence. And those four were restricted by essential non-permanence and were not restricted by non-necessity because non-permanence is more specific than non-necessity; for the absence of permanence entails absence of necessity, without the converse, because it is possible that something is perpetual but not necessary. End of report, ponder. (His words: by essential non-permanence or essential non-necessity) It should be known that necessity is five: the eternal, which is attained eternally and perpetually, like our saying: God, exalted, is knowing by eternal necessity; the essential, i.e. attained as long as the essence of the subject exists; the attribute-based, which is necessity with respect to a description of the subject; the necessity with respect to a time, either definite or indefinite; and the necessity conditioned by the predicate, which is the necessity of affirming the predicate to the subject or negating it from it on condition of affirming the predicate or negating it. And that permanence has three divisions: eternal permanence, which is that the predicate is affirmed of the subject or negated from it eternally and perpetually, like our saying: the celestial sphere is moving with eternal permanence; essential permanence, which is that the predicate is affirmed or negated as long as the essence of the subject exists; and attribute-based permanence, which is that the affirmation or negation lasts as long as the essence of the subject is described by the titular description. This is stated in Sharh al-Matali'. When you know this, then the restriction of negated necessity by the essential is to guard against other than it. And the two distributed absolute (muntashira mutlaqa) are [restricted] by essential non-permanence; i.e. each of these mentioned propositions may be restricted by essential non-permanence. And it is called the universal conditional restricted by non-permanence: ‘the particular conditional’. (It is in the accusative as a second object of 'is called'.) (His words: the particular conditional) The author's apparent meaning is that the particular conditional is the universal conditional in the first sense with the restriction of essential permanence. And some said: it is in the second sense and is restricted by non-permanence as well; i.e. in like: 'Every eclipse is dark as long as it is eclipsed, not perpetually', not in like: 'Every human is an animal, not perpetually.' And similarly for permanence, but the commentator specified the guarded-against as attribute-based necessity and attribute-based permanence. Perhaps that is because what is considered in necessity and permanence is only the essential and attribute-based, not the others; ponder. That is indicated by the fact that essential necessity admits restriction by eternal non-necessity and eternal non-permanence, like: 'Every human is an animal by necessity; not by eternal necessity and not by eternal permanence.' This is a valid compound, but it is not considered, otherwise the compounds would increase greatly by virtue of admitting restriction. (His words: So the universal conditional restricted by non-permanence is called) al-'Isam objected: the universal conditional is that qualified by a single quality only, not that qualified by two qualities; so the intended meaning is what is a universal conditional before restriction by non-permanence, and analogies to it. . . . And what is considered in the concept of the particular conditional is the restriction of the judgment by essential non-permanence, because it is what is considered in . . . (His words: also, etc.) i.e. as it is restricted by essential permanence, and this statement and what preceded it are far-fetched, rather they have no meaning, because the author intends to call the particular conditional the universal conditional; i.e. before restriction by non-permanence, not after it. And similarly for the rest. And I found in the expression of al-'Isam after presenting this observation what supports me in this intended meaning, so I praised God. End. al-Sharnūbī.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the classification of modal propositions, specifically how they are restricted (taqyeed) by the qualifications of non-permanence (la dawam) and non-necessity (la darura). The commentator explains why only the universal conditional (mashruta 'amma) and the two temporal (waqtiyya) propositions are restricted by both qualifications, while the absolute perpetual (da'ima mutlaqa) and the conventional ('urfiyya) are restricted by only one. The reason is that la dawam is more specific than la darura: the absence of permanence necessarily entails the absence of necessity, but not vice versa. For example, 'Every Zanjī is black' is perpetual but not necessary, so it cannot be said 'not by necessity'. The commentator then refines this by clarifying that the intended restrictions are by essential non-permanence (la dawam dhati) and essential non-necessity (la darura dhatiyya), distinguishing these from other types of necessity (eternal, essential, attribute-based, temporal, and conditional) and permanence (eternal, essential, attribute-based). The essential type is chosen because it is the most relevant for guarding against other types. Finally, the passage defines the resulting proposition: the universal conditional restricted by non-permanence is called the particular conditional (mashruta khassa). There is a disagreement about whether the particular conditional is the universal conditional in the first sense restricted by essential permanence, or in the second sense. The commentator sides with the view that it is the universal conditional before restriction, as clarified by al-'Isam and al-Sharnūbī.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is already perpetual by definition, so restricting it by non-permanence would be contradictory.
B) Because non-permanence is more specific than non-necessity, and the perpetual proposition is already necessary.
C) Because the perpetual proposition does not admit restriction by any modality.
D) Because the absolute perpetual is always true, so it cannot be qualified.

Key Takeaway
Essential non-permanence (la-dawam dhati) is more specific than essential non-necessity (la-darura dhatiyya), and this determines which modal propositions can be restricted by each.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition and Composition of the Special Conditional Proposition
Author defines the special conditional proposition and breaks it into universal conditional and absolute negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two components of the special conditional proposition and construct examples of its affirmative and negative forms.

Core Idea
The special conditional is a conditional with a built-in negative to deny permanence.

In this lesson, we will examine a passage from the Tadhheeb that defines the special conditional proposition. You will learn how this compound proposition combines a universal conditional with an absolute negative, and why this structure is essential for expressing non-permanent necessity. Understanding this is crucial for analyzing modal arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find 'المشروطة الخاصة' in the Arabic. Note that it is a type of conditional proposition that asserts necessity under a condition.
2
Extract the two components for the affirmative form
The text states: 'فتركيبها من : مشروطة عامة موجبة، وهي الجزء الأول. ومطلقة عامة سالبة، وهي مفهوم اللادوام.' So the first part is a universal affirmative conditional; the second is a universal absolute negative.
3
Understand the negative component (al-la-dawam)
The negation is not explicit in the conditional but implied by 'not permanently'. The author explains that the affirmation of the predicate for the subject is not permanent, so the negation holds in general.
This negative component is the 'universal absolute negative' (al-mutlaqa al-‘amma al-saliba).
4
Examine the negative form
For the negative special conditional, it is composed of a universal negative conditional (الجزء الأول) and the same absolute negative.
Example from text: 'Necessarily, no writer is still-fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently.'
5
Apply the definition to an Islamic science example
Construct your own example of an affirmative special conditional, e.g., from fiqh: 'Necessarily, every contract is binding as long as both parties are eligible, not permanently.' Identify its two components.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالمشروطة الخاصَّةُ إنْ كانَتْ موجبة، كقولنا : «بالضَّرورة كلُّ كاتب متحرّك الأصابع ما دام كاتباً لا دائماً»؛ فتركيبها من : مشروطة عامة موجبة، وهي الجزء الأول. ومطلقة عامة سالبة، وهي مفهوم اللادوام. لأنَّ إيجابَ المحمول للموضوع إذا لم يكن دائماً ؛ كانَ السَّلبُ في الجملة، وهي معنى المطلقة العامَّةِ السَّالبة؛ أي: كَقولنا : «لا شيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالفعل. وإن كانت سالبةً، كَقولِنَا : بالضّرورة لا شيء من الكاتبِ بساكن الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً لا دائماً»؛ فتركيبها من : سالبة مشروطة عامة هي الجزء الأول.
The special conditional: if it is affirmative, like our saying: 'Necessarily, every writer is moving fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently'; so its composition is: universal affirmative conditional, which is the first part, and universal absolute negative, which is the concept of non-permanence. Because the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, if it is not permanent, then the negation is in general, and that is the meaning of the universal absolute negative; i.e., like our saying: 'No writer is moving fingers in actuality.' And if it is negative, like our saying: 'Necessarily, no writer is still-fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently'; so its composition is: universal negative conditional, which is the first part.
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage defines the special conditional proposition (al-mashruta al-khassa) and explains its logical composition. The author first presents the affirmative form with the example: 'Necessarily, every writer is moving fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently.' This proposition asserts that under the condition of being a writer, the writer necessarily moves his fingers, but this necessity is not permanent; it only holds while the condition lasts. The author then breaks this into two parts: a universal affirmative conditional (the first part: 'Every writer is moving fingers as long as he is a writer') and a universal absolute negative (the second part: the concept of non-permanence, which is expressed as 'No writer is moving fingers permanently' or 'No writer is moving fingers when not writing'). The text explains that the affirmation of the predicate for the subject is not permanent, therefore the negation is true in some sense, which is the meaning of the universal absolute negative. Then the negative form is presented: 'Necessarily, no writer is still-fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently,' which also splits into a universal negative conditional and the same absolute negative. This analysis is crucial for understanding modal syllogisms in logic, particularly how conditional propositions are constructed. In Islamic logic, such distinctions are used to evaluate arguments in fiqh and kalam, for example, when discussing necessary and contingent attributes of actions.

Key Terms
المشروطة الخاصة
المطلقة العامة السالبة
اللادوام

Determining the Quality of Compound Propositions
Author explains rule for determining quality of compound propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a compound proposition is affirmative or negative based on the first part alone, using examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
In compound propositions, only the first part decides the quality.

When a proposition is composed of two parts, how do we know if the whole is affirmative or negative? The author of this passage clarifies that the answer lies entirely in the first part. The second part, which typically expresses non-permanence (la dawam), does not directly affect the quality. This principle is essential for correctly analyzing compound propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts
A compound proposition has a first part (juz' awwal) that is a full proposition (affirmative or negative) and a second part (juz' thani) that is a condition like 'not always' (la dawam).
Example: 'Every writer is moving fingers as long as he is writing, but not always' – the first part is 'Every writer is moving fingers as long as he is writing' (affirmative), the second is 'not always'.
2
Determine the quality of the first part
Look at the first part: is it affirmative (like 'every writer is moving fingers') or negative (like 'no writer is stationary')? This is what matters.
The passage says: 'the consideration in the affirmation and negation of a compound proposition is by the affirmation and negation of the first part.'
3
Apply the rule
The whole compound proposition takes the quality (affirmative or negative) of the first part. The second part does not change it.
So if the first part is affirmative, the whole is affirmative. If the first part is negative, the whole is negative.
4
Verify with examples
Check the examples from the passage: 'Every writer is stationary of fingers actually' (first part affirmative, whole affirmative). 'No writer is moving fingers actually' (first part negative, whole negative).
The second part in both is implied or explicit 'not always', but does not affect the quality.

- A generally conditioned affirmative, which is the first part.
- A generally absolute negative, which is the concept of non-permanence.
Because if the affirmation of the predicate for the subject is not permanent, then negation occurs in the whole, and it is the meaning of the generally absolute negative; i.e., like our saying: 'No writer is moving fingers actually.'
And if it is negative, like our saying: 'By necessity, no writer is stationary of fingers as long as he is writing, but not always'; so its composition is from:
- A generally conditioned negative, which is the first part.
(His saying: 'And it is the concept of non-permanence...') i.e., the generally absolute negative: the concept of non-permanence.
(His saying: 'Because if affirmation...') is a reason for his saying: ('And it is the concept...'). (His saying: 'If it is not permanent') i.e., with respect to the essence; i.e., its non-permanence is taken from 'not always'.
(His saying: 'In the whole') i.e., actually; i.e., disregarding whether it is necessary or not, always or not.
(His saying: 'And it is the meaning...') i.e., the negation realized in the whole derived from 'not always' is the meaning of the generally absolute negative.
(His saying: 'I.e., like our saying: 'Nothing') is an explanation of the generally absolute negative.
(His saying: 'Actually') i.e., upon negating the attribute; i.e., writing.
(His saying: 'And it is the concept of non-permanence; if he said: 'And it is the second part' instead of this saying, it would be better; because the generally absolute negative is not the concept of non-permanence, and what is meant by its concept is our saying: 'No writer is moving fingers actually', so this proposition is composed of these two propositions; because the second part refers to the absolute negative.

- And a generally absolute affirmative, which is the concept of non-permanence.
Because if the negation of the predicate from the subject is not permanent, then affirmation occurs in the whole, and it is the meaning of the generally absolute affirmative; i.e., like our saying: 'Every writer is stationary of fingers actually.'
And from here it becomes clear that the consideration in the affirmation and negation of a compound proposition is by the affirmation and negation of the first part. So if the first part is affirmative, the proposition is affirmative; and if it is negative, the proposition is negative.
(His saying: 'And it is the meaning...') i.e., the affirmation realized in the whole is the meaning... etc. (His saying: 'And from here') i.e., this explanation.
(His saying: 'By the affirmation of the first part...') i.e., not by the affirmation of the second part and its negation. And his saying: 'As was mentioned in the particular conditioned' i.e., but replacing necessity with permanence, like our saying: 'Always every writer is moving fingers as long as he is writing, but not always', and 'Always no writer is stationary of fingers as long as he is writing, but not always'.
(His saying: 'And from here') i.e., because of this detail and clarification it becomes clear... etc. And this is a response to what might be said: 'The reality of a compound proposition is composed of affirmation and negation, so how can it be described as simply affirmative or negative? For a composite of two different things cannot be one of them.' (His saying: 'That the consideration...') i.e., that the criterion in affirmation and negation is on what is actually the case from the two propositions, and the second part is the summary matter in which there is no actual affirmation or negation; rather, if separated, affirmation or negation appears.
(1) (The commentator's saying: 'And from here...') He means that the particular conditioned is composed of two propositions: affirmative and negative, and it is not correct to describe it with both together; rather, it is described only by the first part as affirmative or negative because its quality is apparent. As for the second part, which is non-permanence, the negation or affirmation in it is by implication. And the same is said for the rest. End of the Sharishi note.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section from a logical commentary, dealing with compound propositions and how to determine their quality. The author begins by distinguishing two types: 'generally conditioned affirmative' and 'generally absolute negative'. The first is the first part of a compound proposition; the second is the concept of non-permanence. He explains that if the affirmation of the predicate is not permanent, negation occurs in the whole (i.e., the compound is negative). For example, 'No writer is moving fingers actually' is a general absolute negative. Conversely, if the negation is not permanent, affirmation occurs, as in 'Every writer is stationary of fingers actually'. The key rule is that the quality (affirmation or negation) of the entire compound proposition depends only on the first part. The commentary then addresses an objection: how can a compound of affirmation and negation be described as simply one or the other? The response is that the second part is not actually affirmed or negated in itself; it only implies affirmation or negation when considered separately. This corresponds to the logical principle that in a compound proposition like 'All A are B as long as they are A, but not permanently', the first part ('All A are B') determines the quality, while the second part ('not permanently') is a condition that does not directly affect the quality. The passage also notes that this same reasoning applies to other types of compound propositions, such as the particular conditioned (al-mashruta al-khassa).

مطلقة عامة
جزء أول

Quality of Compound Propositions
Author and commentators explain compound proposition quality rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the quality (affirmative or negative) of any compound proposition in Islamic logic based on its first part.

Core Idea
The quality of a compound proposition is determined solely by its first part.

This passage clarifies a key rule for classifying compound propositions (al-qadiyya al-murakkaba) in Islamic logic. The main text states unequivocally that the overall affirmation or negation follows the first component. The commentators, al-Dasuqi and al-Attar, expand on why this is the case, addressing a potential objection that a compound of affirmation and negation cannot be simply one or the other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound proposition
Look for a proposition that combines two simple propositions, such as a conditional or conjunctive. Note the first part (al-juz' al-awwal) and the second part.
For example: 'If the sun rises, then the day comes' – the first part is 'the sun rises'.
2
Determine the quality of the first part
Is the first part affirmative (موجبة) or negative (سالبة)? The presence of a negation word (e.g., 'ليس', 'لا') indicates negativity.
If the first part is affirmative, the whole is affirmative; if negative, the whole is negative.
3
Apply the rule: quality follows the first part
According to the text, if the first part is affirmative, the entire proposition is affirmative; if negative, the whole is negative. The second part does not affect the classification.
Al-Dasuqi explicitly states that consideration is not given to the second part.
4
Understand the justification from al-Attar
Al-Attar explains that this rule answers an objection: a compound of affirmation and negation is not a simple mixture; the first part dominates, so the whole can be classified by it.
This ensures logical precision in syllogistic reasoning.

And from here it becomes clear that the consideration in the affirmation and negation of the compound proposition is by the affirmation and negation of the first part. So if the first part is affirmative, the proposition is affirmative; and if it is negative, it is negative. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'And from here') i.e., from this explanation. Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the rule for classifying compound propositions. The main text states that the overall quality (positive or negative) of a compound proposition is determined by the first part (al-juz' al-awwal). If the first part is positive, the whole proposition is positive; if negative, the whole is negative. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that the consideration is not based on the second part. Al-Attar then explains that this is a response to an objection: how can a compound of affirmation and negation be called simply affirmative or negative? The answer is that the composition is not a simple mixture; the first part dominates, much like how a mixture of two substances may take the color of one. In the Islamic sciences, this rule is applied to conditional propositions (e.g., 'Every writer is moving his fingers, but not always' – the first part is affirmative, so the whole is affirmative). This logical precision ensures proper classification in syllogistic reasoning.

الجزء الأول

Key Takeaway

The Specific Common Conditional (al-'Urfiyya al-Khassa)
Defines the specific common conditional and its composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the specific common conditional, identify its two component propositions, and explain how the apparent contradiction between 'always' and 'not always' is resolved by the distinction between description and essence.

Core Idea
A compound conditional where 'always' applies by description, 'not always' by essence.

In this lesson, we examine a compound proposition known as the specific common conditional (al-‘urfiyya al-khassa). The text defines it as composed of a general common conditional and an absolute general proposition, each contributing a different aspect of permanence. The commentator Al-Dasuqi then addresses a potential contradiction: how can the same proposition say both 'always' and 'not always'? The answer lies in distinguishing between the description (bi-hasab al-wasf) and the essence (bi-hasab al-dhat). This distinction is a key tool in Islamic logic for constructing precise compound propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two component propositions
The specific common conditional is composed of the general common conditional (al-‘urfiyya al-‘amma) and the absolute general proposition (al-mutlaqa al-‘amma). The former states that something holds always under a certain description; the latter states that it does not hold always when considered in essence.
The absolute general proposition here represents the concept of non-permanence (al-la dawam).
2
Understand the roles of 'always' and 'not always'
The first part of the compound says 'always' (da’iman) with respect to the description, while the second part says 'not always' (la da’iman) with respect to the essence. These are not contradictory because they refer to different aspects.
For example, 'Every writer is moving his fingers always as long as he is a writer (by description), but not always in essence (apart from the description)'.
3
Resolve the apparent contradiction
Al-Dasuqi raises the objection that 'always' and 'not always' seem to conflict. The answer is that 'always' is by virtue of the description, and 'not always' is by virtue of the essence alone. They apply under different conditions, so no contradiction exists.
This resolution is essential for understanding such compound conditionals in Islamic logic.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take the example given: 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, but not always in essence.' Recognize that the first part (general common conditional) states the permanence under the description of being a writer, and the second part (absolute general) negates permanence when considering the essence of the person without the description.
Practice identifying similar examples from Islamic sciences.

Source Text

فَصْلٌ: فِي الْمَعْرِفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
وَالْجُزْءُ الثَّانِي مُخَالِفٌ لِلْجُزْءِ الْأَوَّلِ فِي الْكَيْفِ؛ أَيْ: الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ، وَمُوَافِقٌ لَهُ فِي الْكَمِّ؛ أَيْ: الْكُلِّيَّةِ وَالْجُزْئِيَّةِ، وَسَيَجِيءُ لِهَذَا زِيَادَةُ تَحْقِيقٍ.
وَمِثَالُ الْعُرْفِيَّةِ الْخَاصَّةِ إِيجَاباً وَسَلْباً مَا مَرَّ فِي الْمَشْرُوطَةِ الْخَاصَّةِ. وَتَرْكِيبُهَا مِنَ الْعُرْفِيَّةِ الْعَامَّةِ وَالْمُطْلَقَةِ الْعَامَّةِ، الَّتِي هِيَ مَفْهُومُ اللَّادَوَامِ كَمَا عَرَفْتَ.
الدسوقي: وَفِيهِ أَنَّ آخِرَ الْكَلَامِ يُنَافِي أَوَّلَهُ؛ لِأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ: (لَا دَائِماً) يُنَافِي قَوْلَهُ قَبْلُ: (دَائِماً)، وَأُجِيبُ: بِأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ: (دَائِماً) أَيْ بِحَسَبِ الْوَصْفِ، وَقَوْلَهُ: (لَا دَائِماً) أَيْ بِحَسَبِ الذَّاتِ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الْوَصْفِ، وَقِسْ عَلَيْهِ نَظَائِرَهُ كَمَا يَأْتِي فِي الشَّارِحِ.
Chapter: On the Definition and Its Divisions.
And the second part is opposite to the first part in quality; i.e., affirmation and negation, and agrees with it in quantity; i.e., universality and particularity, and further investigation of this will come.
And the example of the specific common conditional, both positive and negative, is what passed in the specific conditional. And its composition is from the general common conditional and the absolute general proposition, which is the concept of non-permanence as you know.
Al-Dasuqi: And in it [there is an issue] that the end of the statement contradicts its beginning; because his saying 'not always' contradicts his saying before 'always', and it is answered: that his saying 'always' is with respect to the description, and his saying 'not always' is with respect to the essence, disregarding the description. And analogize to it its counterparts as will come in the commentator.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage defines the specific common conditional (al-‘urfiyya al-khassa) and its structure. The main text states that the second part of this compound proposition differs from the first in quality (one positive, one negative) but agrees in quantity (both universal or both particular). An example is similar to the specific conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa) but with 'always' and 'not always'. The specific common conditional is composed of a general common conditional (al-‘urfiyya al-‘amma) and an absolute general proposition (al-mutlaqa al-‘amma), where the latter represents the concept of non-permanence (la dawam). Al-Dasuqi raises an objection: in the example, saying 'always' and then 'not always' seems contradictory. The answer distinguishes: 'always' is with respect to the description (e.g., as long as one is a writer, he is moving fingers always), while 'not always' is with respect to the essence (e.g., considering the essence without the description, it is not always). This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction and is a key logical tool for handling such compound conditionals. In Islamic logic, this is used for propositions like 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, but not always in essence'.

The Special Customary Proposition: Definition and Composition
Definition and composition of the special customary proposition with objection and answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a special customary proposition and explain how the apparent contradiction between 'permanent' and 'not permanent' is resolved through the essential-vs-descriptive distinction.

Core Idea
The special customary proposition harmonizes permanent attribute with non-permanent essence.

In this passage, the author defines the special customary proposition (العرفية الخاصة), a key type of proposition in Islamic logic that combines a general customary and a general absolute. He also tackles an apparent contradiction: the definition seems to say the proposition is both 'permanent' and 'not permanent'. As we work through the passage, you will learn how the author resolves this by distinguishing truth by attribute (descriptive) from truth by essence. This distinction is crucial for understanding modal propositions used in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find where the author states what the special customary proposition is composed of. The passage says it is composed of the general customary and the general absolute, where the general absolute is the concept of non-permanence (lā dawām).
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that the definition contains a contradiction: it includes 'not permanent' (from the general absolute) but earlier (in the special conditional) it had 'permanent'. Identify the exact phrases that seem to conflict.
The objection is signaled by 'وفيه : أنَّ آخِرَ الكلام يُنافي أوَّلَهُ'.
3
Extract the resolution
The answer distinguishes two respects: 'permanent' refers to the attribute (writing), while 'not permanent' refers to the essence (when the attribute is disregarded). Thus the proposition is true permanently with respect to the attribute but not permanently with respect to the essence.
The key distinction is between dhātī (essential) and waṣfī (descriptive).
4
Apply the distinction to similar cases
The author says 'measure its parallels accordingly' – meaning other special customary propositions follow the same pattern. Practice constructing an example from Fiqh or Kalam where a proposition is true as long as a certain attribute holds but not always by essence.
For example: 'Every writer moves his hand' – true while writing (descriptive), but not true if he stops writing (essential).

Why Non-Permanence Must Be Essential in Special Propositions
Author explains restriction of non-permanence to essential in special propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential non-permanence from descriptive non-permanence and explain why special propositions require the former.

Core Idea
Special propositions already have descriptive permanence, so their non-permanence must be essential.

The special conditional and special customary are defined by restricting their general counterparts with non-permanence. But why must that non-permanence be essential rather than descriptive? The author clarifies that the general versions already imply permanence by attribute, so descriptive non-permanence is impossible. Understanding this nuance prevents a common confusion in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two special propositions
Recall that the special conditional (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa) and special customary ('urfiyya khāṣṣa) are derived from the general conditional and general customary by adding a restriction of non-permanence (lā dawām).
The general propositions are permanent with respect to an attribute.
2
Understand the two types of non-permanence
Essential non-permanence (lā dawām dhātī) negates permanence regarding the subject's essence, while descriptive non-permanence (lā dawām waṣfī) negates permanence regarding an attribute.
These are distinct from each other.
3
Analyze why descriptive non-permanence is impossible
The general customary has explicit descriptive permanence: it holds as long as the attribute exists. The general conditional is a necessity relative to an attribute, so it also entails descriptive permanence. Thus, applying descriptive non-permanence would contradict their inherent nature.
The passage states: 'in each of them there is permanence according to the attribute'.
4
Conclude the necessity of essential non-permanence
Since descriptive non-permanence is ruled out, the only remaining option is essential non-permanence. Therefore, both special propositions must be defined with essential non-permanence.
This ensures consistency in definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنَّما قيَّد اللادوامَ فيهما بالذَّاتي ؛ لأنَّ المشروطة الخاصة - على ما عرفتها - هي المشروطة العامة المقيدة باللادوام. والعرفيّةُ الخاصة هي العرفية العامة المقيّدة به أيضاً ويمتنع تقييد المشروطة والعرفية العامتين باللادوام الوصفي؛ إذْ في كلّ واحدة منهما دوام بحسب الوصف، أما العرفية العامَّةُ فظاهرة، وأمَّا المشروطة العامَّةُ ؛ فلأنَّها ضرورةٌ بحسب الوصف، فتكون دواماً الوصف لا محالة .
And he only restricted non-permanence in both of them by the essential because the special conditional, as you have known, is the general conditional restricted by non-permanence. And the special customary is the general customary also restricted by it. And it is impossible to restrict the general conditional and general customary by descriptive non-permanence, because in each of them there is permanence according to the attribute. As for the general customary, it is apparent; and as for the general conditional, because it is necessity according to the attribute, so it becomes permanence of the attribute necessarily.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
Here the author explains why non-permanence (lā dawām) in both special propositions (the special conditional and the special customary) is restricted by the essential (dhātī) rather than the descriptive (waṣfī). The special conditional is defined as the general conditional (sharṭiyya 'āmma) restricted by non-permanence; similarly, the special customary is the general customary ('urfiyya 'āmma) restricted by non-permanence. Restricting the general versions by descriptive non-permanence is impossible because each already involves permanence with respect to an attribute. The general customary explicitly has permanence as long as the attribute exists; the general conditional is a necessity with respect to an attribute, which necessarily entails permanence of that attribute. Thus, one cannot apply descriptive non-permanence. Therefore, the restriction must be essential, i.e., non-permanence with respect to the essence itself, disregarding attributes.

Restricting Specific Conditionals: Essential vs Descriptive Non-Permanence
Author rationalises the restriction of specific conditionals with essential non-permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential and descriptive non-permanence and explain why descriptive non-permanence cannot restrict general conditionals.

Core Idea
Essential non-permanence restricts the subject's essence; descriptive non-permanence would contradict descriptive permanence.

In this passage, the author clarifies why the specific conditional (المشروطة الخاصة) and the specific customary (العرفية الخاصة) must be restricted by essential non-permanence (اللادوام الذاتي) rather than descriptive non-permanence (اللادوام الوصفي). The key insight is that general conditionals already involve necessity or permanence according to description, so adding descriptive non-permanence would create a contradiction. This distinction is critical for correctly classifying propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of specific propositions
Recognise that the specific conditional and specific customary are defined as the general conditional and general customary restricted by non-permanence. The question is: which kind of non-permanence?
The author says: 'the specific conditional... is the general conditional restricted by non-permanence.'
2
Understand the two kinds of non-permanence
Essential non-permanence (اللادوام الذاتي) means the proposition does not hold for all individuals of the subject essence. Descriptive non-permanence (اللادوام الوصفي) means it does not hold for all individuals as long as the description (e.g., being a writer) holds.
The key difference: essence vs. description.
3
Analyse why descriptive non-permanence is impossible
The general conditional involves necessity according to description (ضرورة بحسب الوصف), which implies descriptive permanence. Similarly, the general customary has apparent permanence according to description. Adding descriptive non-permanence would directly contradict this inherent descriptive permanence.
Thus, only essential non-permanence is a valid restriction.
4
Extend the same reasoning to other proposition types
The author notes that absolute temporal (الوقتية المطلقة) and absolute distributed (المنتشرة المطلقة) propositions are similarly restricted by time, which functions like a descriptive restriction. Hence, they also cannot be restricted by descriptive non-permanence.
Time-based restrictions resemble descriptive restrictions.
5
Formulate the final rule
If a valid restriction is needed, it must be by essential non-permanence. The judgment of necessity or permanence according to description is then qualified by non-permanence with respect to the essence of the subject.
This yields the correct definitions of the specific propositions.

وإنَّما قيَّد اللادوامَ فيهما بالذَّاتي ; لأنَّ المشروطة الخاصة - على ما عرفتها - هي المشروطة العامة المقيدة باللادوام. والعرفيّةُ الخاصة هي العرفية العامة المقيّدة به أيضاً. ويمتنع تقييد المشروطة والعرفية العامتين باللادوام الوصفي؛ إذْ في كلّ واحدة منهما دوام بحسب الوصف، أما العرفية العامَّةُ فظاهرة، وأمَّا المشروطة العامَّةُ ; فلأنَّها ضرورةٌ بحسب الوصف، فتكون دواماً وصف لا محالة. ومثل ذلك يُقالُ في الوقتيَّةِ المطلقة، والمنتشرة المطلقة؛ إذ التَّقييدُ بِالوقتِ فيهما بمنزلةِ التَّقييدِ بِالوَصْفِ. ويمتنع أن يقيَّد باللادوام الوصفي، بل إذا أُريد تقييده بقيد صحيح؛ فلا بد أن يقيَّد باللادوام الذاتي، ويكون الحكم حينئذ بضرورة النّسبة أو دوامها بحسب الوصف مقيَّداً باللادوام بحسَبِ الذَّاتِ.
He only restricted the non-permanence in both with the essential; because the specific conditional, as you have known it, is the general conditional restricted by non-permanence. And the specific customary is the general customary also restricted by it. And it is impossible to restrict the general conditional and general customary with descriptive non-permanence, since in each of them there is permanence according to description; as for the general customary it is apparent, and as for the general conditional, because it is necessity according to description, thus it is necessarily descriptive permanence. And similar is said regarding the absolute temporal and the absolute distributed; for the restriction by time in both is like the restriction by description. And it is impossible to restrict it with descriptive non-permanence; rather, if one intends to restrict it with a valid restriction, then it must be restricted with essential non-permanence, and the ruling then of the necessity of the relation or its permanence according to description is restricted by non-permanence with respect to essence.

Commentary
This passage explains the rationale behind restricting specific conditional and specific customary propositions with essential non-permanence (اللادوام الذاتي) rather than descriptive non-permanence (اللادوام الوصفي). The author begins by stating that the specific conditional is defined as the general conditional restricted by non-permanence. Similarly, the specific customary is the general customary restricted by the same. Then he argues that it is impossible to restrict the general conditional and general customary propositions with descriptive non-permanence because each of them inherently involves permanence based on description. The general customary proposition has apparent permanence as long as the subject's description exists. The general conditional proposition is a necessity according to description, which necessarily implies permanence according to description. Therefore, adding descriptive non-permanence would create a contradiction. The same reasoning applies to absolute temporal (الوقتية المطلقة) and absolute distributed (المنتشرة المطلقة) propositions, where the restriction by time functions like a descriptive restriction. Consequently, any valid restriction must be by essential non-permanence, and the judgment of necessity or permanence according to description is then qualified by non-permanence with respect to the essence of the subject.

The Temporal Proposition: Definition and Composition
Author defines temporal proposition composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two components of an affirmative and a negative temporal proposition and correctly compose them.

Core Idea
The temporal proposition adds a time condition and a non-permanence qualifier.

In this lesson, we examine the temporal proposition (al-waqtiyya), a compound modal proposition that expresses a judgment true only during a specific time. The author uses lunar eclipse examples to illustrate affirmative and negative forms. Understanding its composition is essential for analyzing modal arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the temporal proposition is affirmative or negative. The passage gives examples: affirmative 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of interposition, not permanently' and negative 'No moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not permanently'.
2
Extract the first part (al-juz' al-awwal)
The first part is the temporal absolute: for affirmative, it is 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of interposition'; for negative, 'No moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature'.
This part is the temporal absolute, either affirmative or negative.
3
Extract the second part (concept of non-permanence)
The second part expresses that the judgment is not permanent. For affirmative, it is 'not permanently' which is a universal negative absolute. For negative, it is also 'not permanently' but expressed as an affirmative absolute because the first part is negative.
Al-ladawam (non-permanence) is the key condition.
4
Verify the composition
Check that the two parts together correctly capture the temporal restriction: the judgment holds at the specified time but not always. The non-permanence qualifier prevents the proposition from being universally true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوقتيَّةُ؛ فهي إن كانت موجبة، كَقولنا بالضرورة: «كل قمرٍ منخسِفُ وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبينَ الشَّمس لا دائماً»؛ فتركيبها من : موجبة وقتية مطلقة الجزء الأول. وسالبة مُطلقة عامة هي مفهوم اللادوام. وإن كانت سالبة كَقولنا : بالضَّرورة لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لا دائماً»؛ فتركيبها من : سالبة وقتية مطلقة هي الجزء الأوَّل . وموجبة مطلقة هي مفهوم اللادوام.
As for the temporal (al-waqtiyya): if it is affirmative, like our saying necessarily: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, not permanently'; so its composition is: an affirmative temporal absolute is the first part, and a universal negative absolute is the concept of non-permanence. And if it is negative, like our saying necessarily: 'No moon is eclipsed at the time of the quadrature, not permanently'; so its composition is: a negative temporal absolute is the first part, and an affirmative absolute is the concept of non-permanence.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage defines the temporal proposition (al-waqtiyya), which is one of the compound modal propositions. The author explains that the temporal proposition can be either affirmative or negative, and each has a specific composition. For the affirmative temporal proposition, the example is: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, not permanently.' This means that the eclipse occurs only at that specific time, but not always. Its composition consists of two parts: first, an affirmative temporal absolute (the affirmative temporal part), and second, a universal negative absolute which is the concept of non-permanence (al-ladawam). The non-permanence condition ensures that the proposition is not true at all times. For the negative temporal proposition, the example is: 'No moon is eclipsed at the time of the quadrature, not permanently.' This means that the moon is not eclipsed at the quadrature, but it is not permanently the case that it is not eclipsed (i.e., it could be eclipsed at other times). Its composition is: a negative temporal absolute as the first part, and an affirmative absolute as the concept of non-permanence. The example uses the lunar eclipse, a well-known phenomenon in Islamic astronomy, to illustrate the temporal condition. The 'time of interposition' refers to when the Earth is between the sun and the moon, causing an eclipse, while 'quadrature' is when the moon is at a right angle to the sun, so no eclipse occurs. The temporal proposition thus captures a truth that holds only during a specific time interval.

Temporal and Distributed Propositions: Definitions and Key Distinctions
Author defines temporal and distributed propositions with commentary on time specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish temporal from distributed propositions by their time specification and composition, and correctly interpret the term 'specific time' in modal logic.

Core Idea
Temporal propositions judge necessity in a specific time; distributed judge in indefinite time, both with non-permanence.

In this lesson, we examine two types of modal propositions: temporal (waqtiyya) and distributed (muntashira). These classifications are not abstract—they underpin arguments in kalam about the world's creation and in fiqh about time-bound obligations. Understanding their structure will allow you to identify the logical force of statements that depend on time qualifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of a temporal proposition
Look for necessity in a specific time, restricted by non-permanence (lādawām). The judgment holds necessarily but only within a particular time frame, not perpetually.
Example: 'Necessarily, every Muslim fasts during Ramadan during daylight hours' – fasting is necessary in that specific time, but not always.
2
Understand the composition of temporal propositions
An affirmative temporal proposition combines an absolute distributed affirmative (the first part) with an absolute universal negative representing non-permanence. For a negative temporal, it's an absolute distributed negative plus an absolute universal affirmative.
This composition reflects that the judgment is necessary in a specific time and absent outside it.
3
Interpret 'specific time' correctly
Al-Isam clarifies that 'specific time' means a type of time more specific than any time of the subject's existence, not a particular moment. This excludes particular conditional propositions where the time is an individual moment.
The time can be multiple specific times, as long as they are more specific than any time of the subject's existence.
4
Distinguish the distributed proposition
The distributed proposition has the same structure but the time is indefinite (ghayr mu'ayyan). The judgment is necessary in some indefinite time, not always.
Example: 'Necessarily, every human is breathing in some time' – the time is vague, not specified.
5
Apply the definitions to the given examples
The passage gives: 'Necessarily, every human is breathing in some time, not always' – this is a distributed proposition because the time is indefinite. Compare with a temporal example: 'Necessarily, every Muslim fasts during Ramadan during daylight hours' – that is temporal.
Practise by converting the distributed example into a temporal one by specifying a time (e.g., 'in the first moment of life').

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The temporal proposition is that in which it is judged with necessity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject, or its negation, in a specific time from the times of the existence of the subject, restricted by non-permanence with respect to the essence. Its composition is from: an absolute distributed affirmative proposition as the first part, and an absolute universal negative proposition which is the concept of non-permanence, if it is affirmative. And from: an absolute distributed negative proposition, and an absolute universal affirmative proposition which is the concept of non-permanence, if it is negative. Al-Dusuqi, al-Attar, al-Mudaf. (His saying: 'in a specific time' - al-Isam said: what is meant is the specification of that which is more specific than any time from the times of the existence of the subject, not the individual specification. And whoever said: what is meant is the time, it is objected against him that some times of the essence are added, and the proposition does not become temporal by it. And it is necessary that by 'specific time' be intended what includes the single time and the multiple, to include in the definition the temporal proposition restricted by multiple specific times, and that the specific time be intended without the descriptive attribute to exclude the particular conditional proposition from the definition. (His saying: 'in a non-specific time' - what is meant by it is what includes the multiple, so it includes the definition of the distributed proposition restricted by multiple ambiguous times; said al-Isam.) Commentary
This passage defines two types of modal propositions: the temporal (al-waqtiyya) and the distributed (al-muntashira). It begins with the temporal proposition, which is one where the judgment of the predicate to the subject is made with necessity, but only in a specific time period of the subject's existence, and this necessity is qualified by non-permanence (al-lādawām) relative to the essence. The author then gives the composition of the temporal proposition: for an affirmative temporal proposition, it is composed of an absolute distributed affirmative (the first part) and an absolute universal negative that represents the concept of non-permanence; for a negative temporal proposition, it is composed of an absolute distributed negative and an absolute universal affirmative. These names (Dusuqi, Attar, Mudaf) indicate marginal commentators. Next, the passage presents a commentary note on the phrase 'in a specific time.' Al-Isam clarifies that 'specific' here means a type of time that is more specific than any particular time of the subject's existence—not a specific moment like '3:00 PM.' This is to avoid the temporal proposition becoming merely relative. The note also explains that 'in a non-specific time' in the distributed proposition includes multiple indefinite times. Finally, the passage gives an example: 'Necessarily, every human is breathing in some time, not always' (affirmative) and its negative counterpart. This example illustrates the distributed proposition, as the time is indefinite ('some time') and the mode is necessary but not permanent.

Definition of the Existential Non-Necessary Proposition
Author defines the existential non-necessary proposition, explains its composition, and discusses when restriction with non-necessity is valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the existential non-necessary proposition, explain its composition (absolute general affirmative + general possible negative), and state the condition that it cannot be formed when the predicate is necessary for the subject.

Core Idea
The existential non-necessary proposition asserts actual existence without necessity.

In this lesson, we examine a specific type of modal proposition: the existential non-necessary (al-wujūdiyya al-lāḍarūriyya). It is formed by restricting the absolute general proposition with the mode of non-necessity of essence. The author explains when such a restriction is valid and gives its composition. Understanding this proposition is essential for analyzing arguments that involve existence and modality in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base proposition
The base is the absolute general proposition (al-muṭlaqa al-ʿāmma), an unmodalized universal affirmative or negative, e.g., 'Every human is laughing' (without any qualification).
This base can be actual, necessary, or possible.
2
Understand the restriction with non-necessity
The absolute general proposition is restricted by 'non-necessity of essence' (al-lāḍarūra al-dhātiyya), meaning the judgment holds actually but not necessarily with respect to the essence of the subject.
The mode 'lāḍarūra' modifies the proposition, turning it into a modal proposition.
3
Examine the composition of the affirmative form
The affirmative existential non-necessary proposition (e.g., 'Every human is laughing actually, not necessarily') is composed of two parts: (1) an absolute general affirmative ('Every human is laughing') and (2) a general possible negative ('some humans may not laugh' or 'it is not necessary').
The second part negates the necessity, not the actuality.
4
Determine the condition for validity
Restriction with non-necessity is only valid when the predicate is not necessary for the subject. If the predicate is necessary (e.g., 'Every human is an animal'), the restriction would create a contradiction because the absolute general would already be necessary.
Check whether the predicate can be absent without violating the essence of the subject.
5
Apply the definition to examples
Given a proposition, decide if it qualifies as existential non-necessary. For instance, 'Every human is sleeping at some time' is not existential non-necessary because it is distributed; 'Every human is laughing actually' is a candidate if it is not necessary.
The commentary emphasizes that the proposition must assert actual existence of the relation.
