The Negative Conditional: Negation of Connection vs. Negation of Consequent
Commentator defends the definition of the negative conditional against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a true negative conditional from an affirmative modified conditional by locating where the negation operates.

Core Idea
A negative conditional negates the connection, not the consequent.

In conditional logic, the placement of negation determines whether a proposition is truly negative or merely affirmative with a negated part. This lesson unpacks the objection that the definition of the negative conditional is too broad, and the commentator's clarification that a proposition like 'If the sun is risen, then night does not exist' is actually affirmative modified, not a negative conditional. Understanding this distinction is crucial for accurate logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition under attack
Find the author's definition of the negative conditional and understand why the objector thinks it fails. The objector claims that the definition is not exclusive (man') because it seems to include 'If the sun is risen, then night does not exist', which he considers not a true negative conditional.
The definition in question is 'or its negation' from the matn, interpreted as 'or the negation of the affirmation of a relation'.
2
Identify the objector's counterexample
Examine the proposition 'If the sun is risen, then night does not exist'. It has a negation in the consequent, but the commentator argues it is an affirmative modified (mujiba ma'dula) because the negation only affects the consequent, not the connection.
This proposition appears to be a counterexample to the definition's exclusivity, but the commentator shows it is not truly negative.
3
Analyze the commentator's distinction
Note that the negation can attach to the antecedent (making a true negative conditional that negates the connection) or to the consequent (making an affirmative modified). The key is whether the connection itself is negated. The commentator explains that the author's phrase 'or its negation' involves an omitted genitive: 'or the negation of its affirmation', i.e., negation of the connection.
Compare with categoricals: 'Zayd is not standing' is a negative categorical, but 'Zayd is not-not standing' is affirmative modified.
4
Apply to analogous categorical cases
The commentator analogizes to categorical propositions: just as negation in categoricals is about the copula (salb al-haml), not the terms, similarly in conditionals it is about the connection (salb al-ittisal). The four combinations of affirmative/negative extremes occur in both types.
Al-Attar's addition from al-Sayyid emphasizes that the extremes' positivity/negativity does not determine the proposition's quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلَمْ أَنَّ ثبوت نسبةٍ على تقديرِ أُخرَى؛
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ تُبُوْتَ ... إلخ) دفع (۱) بهذا ما يرد على ظاهر كلام المصنف مِنْ أنَّ تعريفهُ لِلسَّالبة غيرُ مانع لصدقِهِ على نحو : إِنْ كَانَتِ الشَّمس طالعة؛ فليس اللَّيلُ موجود، فإن حكم فيه بنفي نسبة؛ أي: أمر على تقديرِ أُخرى، مع أنَّ هذهِ القضيَّةً مُوجبةٌ مَعدولةٌ، وتعريف الموجبة غير جامع لخروج نحو هذا عنه وحاصلُهُ : أنَّ قولَ المصنّف : (أو بنفيها على حذف مضاف؛ أي: أو بنفي ثبوتها؛ أي: أو بنفي ثبوت نسبة؛ أي: أمر على تقدير أُخرى .
.
وتوضيحه : أنَّ أداةَ السَّلْبِ إِنْ دخلَتْ على المقدَّم؛ فالقضيَّةُ سالبة للحكم فيها بِسَلْبِ الاتصال، وإِنْ أَخَّرَ السَّلْبَ إلى التّالي ؛ فَهِيَ مُتَّصلَةُ السَّلْبِ، فهي موجبةٌ لَا سالبةٌ كَمَا قد يتوهم من المصنفِ .
وظاهرُهُ : أَنَّها إذا تأخَّرَ السَّلْبُ فيها لِلتّالي؛ تكون موجبةً قطعاً، مع أنَّها لا تكونُ كَذلِكَ إِلَّا إِذا جُعِلَ حرفُ السَّلْبِ جزءاً من التَّالي، وأَمَّا إِنْ جُعِلَ التَّالي مَا بعدَ النَّفي؛ كانَتْ سالبة، فهي مثل: زيد ليس بقائم، وهذا المثال إشارة إلى أنَّ العدولَ يدخلُ الشَّرطيَّة، بل ويدخلها الموجّهات.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ: وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ ثُبُوْتَ نِسْبَةِ ... إِلَخ) قالَ السَّيِّدُ : كَمَا أَنَّ السَّلْبَ في الحمليَّاتِ بحسبِ سلبِ الحمل ؛ لا باعتبارِ طَرفَيْهَا عدولاً وتحصيلاً ؛ فَرُبَّما كانَ طَرَفَا الحمليَّةِ مُسْتَمِلَتَيْنِ على حرفِ السَّلْبِ، وتكونُ القضيَّةُ مُوجبةٌ ؛ كَذَلِكَ السَّلْبُ في المتصلات والمنفصلاتِ بحسب سَلْبِ الاتِّصالِ ونَوعَيْهِ؛ أعني : اللُّزوم والاتفاق، وبحسبِ سلب الانفصال ونَوعَيْهِ؛ أعني : العناد والاتفاق، وَلَا اعتبار بأطرافِ الشَّرطيَّاتِ في سَلْبِها وإيجابها ؛ بل الأقسام الأربعةُ ؛ أعني : كونَ الطَّرفَين مُوجِبَتَيْنِ وَسالبتَيْنِ، وكونَ المقدَّمِ مُوجباً والتّالي سالباً، وبالعكس يوجد في الموجباتِ والسَّوالب في المتّصلات والمنفصلات .ا.هـ
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : دفع ... إلخ) المعترض لم يفهم أن الثبوت هو الاتصال وأن النفي نفيه، فاعترض كما أنَّه لم يفهم أن المراد بالنسبة : الأمرُ ، عدمياً كان أو وجودياً . ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
And know that the affirmation of a relation upon another condition; Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'And know that the affirmation... etc.') He repels (1) by this what might be raised against the apparent meaning of the author's words, namely that his definition of the negative conditional is not exclusive (man') because it applies to something like: 'If the sun is risen, then night does not exist'; for it judges the negation of a relation, i.e., something upon another condition, while this proposition is an affirmative modified (mujiba ma'dula), and the definition of the affirmative is not comprehensive because it excludes this case. The summary is that the author's statement: ('Or its negation') involves an omitted genitive; i.e., 'or the negation of its affirmation'; i.e., 'or the negation of the affirmation of a relation'; i.e., 'something upon another condition.' And its clarification: that the particle of negation, if it enters upon the antecedent, then the proposition is negative, with judgment therein of the negation of connection; and if it postpones the negation to the consequent, then it is a connected conditional of negation, so it is affirmatively not negative, as might be misunderstood from the author. And its apparent meaning: that if the negation therein is postponed to the consequent, it is definitely affirmative, while it is not like that unless the particle of negation is made part of the consequent; but if the consequent is made to be what is after the negation, then it is negative; it is like: 'Zayd is not standing.' This example is an indication that 'adul' (modification) enters into conditionals, and even enters modal propositions. Al-Attar: (His statement: 'And know that the affirmation of a relation... etc.') As-Sayyid said: Just as negation in categoricals is according to the negation of predication, not with consideration of its two extremes in terms of modification and determination; so perhaps the two extremes of a categorical contain the particle of negation, and the proposition is affirmative; likewise negation in connected and disconnected conditionals is according to the negation of connection and its two types, i.e., absolute relation (luzum) and coincidence (ittifaq), and according to the negation of disconnection and its two types, i.e., opposition ('inad) and coincidence, and no consideration is given to the extremes of conditionals in their negation and affirmation; rather the four divisions, i.e., that the two extremes are both affirmative and both negative, and that the antecedent is affirmative and the consequent is negative, and the opposite, occur in affirmatives and negatives in connected and disconnected conditionals. (1) (His statement: 'He repels... etc.) The objector did not understand that 'affirmation' is the connection and the negation is its negation, so he objected; and similarly he did not understand that by 'relation' is meant the thing, whether non-existential or existential. End.
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage is a critical-dialectical section where the commentator defends the definition of the negative conditional against an objection. The opponent argues that the definition of the negative conditional (saliba) is not exclusive (man') because it would include propositions like 'If the sun is risen, then night does not exist' – which is actually an affirmative modified (mujiba ma'dula) proposition, not a true negative conditional. Consequently, the definition of the affirmative would fail to encompass this case. The commentator's response, attributed to al-Dasuqi, clarifies that the author's words 'or its negation' involve an omitted genitive; the correct interpretation is 'or the negation of its affirmation', i.e., the negation of the affirmation of the relation. He then explains the key distinction: if the negation particle attaches to the antecedent, the proposition is a genuine negative whose judgment negates the connection; if the negation is postponed to the consequent, it is an affirmative proposition of 'negation of the consequent' (muttasilat al-salab) – not a negative conditional. However, this is only true if the negation is part of the consequent; if the consequent is taken as what follows the negation, then it is still negative. He analogizes this to categoricals like 'Zayd is not standing', which is a negative categorical despite containing negation. Al-Attar then adds an important point from al-Sayyid: negation in conditionals, like in categoricals, is determined by the negation of the connection (or disconnection) itself, not by the affirmative or negative status of the antecedent or consequent. The four possible combinations (both affirmative, both negative, antecedent affirmative/consequent negative, and opposite) apply equally to affirmative and negative conditionals. Finally, al-Sharnubi notes that the objector fundamentally misunderstood that 'affirmation' here means the connection (ittisal) and 'negation' means its negation, and that 'relation' refers to the thing (amr) itself, whether existential or non-existential.

Key Terms
سالبة
عدول
اتصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is the proposition 'If the sun is risen, then night does not exist' not a true negative conditional?
A) Because the antecedent is affirmative.
B) Because the negation is postponed to the consequent, making it an affirmative modified conditional.
C) Because it is a categorical proposition disguised as a conditional.
D) Because the connection is not negated; the proposition asserts a disjunction.

Key Takeaway
The defining feature of a negative conditional is the negation of the connection (ittisal), not the presence of negation in the consequent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following proposition as negative conditional or affirmative modified: 'If someone prays, then his prayer is not invalid.' Explain your reasoning using the commentator's distinction.
?
In a debate about the validity of a sale conditional on a third party's approval, a scholar says: 'It is not that if the third party approves, the sale becomes valid.' Is this a denial of the conditional relationship, or something else? Use the commentator's clarification to justify your answer.

Defining Conditional Propositions and Negation of Connection
Author opens section on types of conditionals by defining connection and negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between affirmative and negative conditional propositions by analyzing whether the judgement affirms or negates the connection itself.

Core Idea
A conditional is affirmative if it affirms the connection; negative only if the connection itself is negated.

In this passage, the author opens a new section on the types of conditional propositions. He gives a precise definition: a conditional is an expression of the connection between two relations. The crucial insight is that to determine whether a conditional is affirmative or negative, we must look at whether the judgement itself affirms or negates that connection—not at whether the parts contain negations. The commentators expand on this with an example to prevent a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relations (nisbatayn)
In any conditional, find the two propositions that are connected. For example, in 'If the sun rises, the day comes', the two relations are 'sun rising' and 'day coming'.
These are the contents of the antecedent and consequent.
2
Determine the judgement on the connection
Ask: Does the proposition affirm that the first relation implies or is linked to the second? Or does it deny that link? The judgement is about the connection itself.
The author says: 'the judgement of its negation is an expression of the negation of the connection.'
3
Check the locus of negation if present
If a negation appears, is it part of one of the relations (e.g., 'night is not existent') or is it the judgement on the connection? Only if the judgement itself negates the connection is the conditional negative.
Be careful not to confuse 'connection of negation' (negation inside a relation) with 'negation of connection' (judgement negating the link).
4
Apply to the example
Consider 'If the sun rises, then the night is not existent'. Here the judgement affirms that sunrise implies non-existence of night. The negation is only in the consequent, not in the connection. Hence it is an affirmative conditional.
The commentators emphasize this to correct a potential misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيةِ عبارةٌ عن الاتصال بينَ النِّسبتين، فالحكم بنفيها يكون عبارة عن سَلبِ الاتصال . فالقضيَّةُ السَّالبة هي تحكم فيها بسلب الاتصال، لا باتصالِ السَّلبِ، فإنَّ ما حُكِمَ فيه باتِّصالِ السَّلبِ موجبةٌ لا سالبة . الدسوقي: وذلك لأنَّ النِّسبةَ الَّتي مُحكم بثبوتِهَا أو بنفيها على تقدير أُخرى؛ إما أن تكونَ ضروريَّةً أو دائمةً أو حاصلة بالفعل أو ممكنة ولم يذكرْهُمَا فيها اكتفاء بذكرِهِمَا في الحملية. (قَوْلُهُ : عِبَارَة عَنِ (الاتِّصَالِ) أي: الارتباطِ واللُّزوم، وقوله: (بينَ النِّسبتَينِ)؛ أي : بين الأمرين؛ أعني : مضمونَ التَّالي والمقدَّم. (قَوْلُهُ : فَالْحُكْمُ بِنَفْيِهَا ... إِلَخ). فَنَفيُهَا يكونُ ... إلخ . (قَوْلُهُ: بِسَلْبِ الاتِّصَالِ) أي : ليس وجودُ اللَّيلِ لَازماً لطلوعِ الشَّمسِ، فالاتصال هنا هو اللزوم، وليس المراد أن تكونَ التَّاليةُ مُتَّصلَةٌ بِالأُوْلَى؛ أي: ليس فاصلاً بينهما . العطار: (قَوْلُهُ: فَالْحُكُمُ بِنَفْيِهَا يَكُونُ ... إِلَخ) أي : الحكم بنفي نسبة على تقدير أُخرى ... إلخ، وغرضُهُ مِن هذا دفعُ مَا يُتوهَّمُ مِن كلام المصنفِ مِن أَنَّ مثلَ : إِنْ كَانَتِ الشَّمسُ طالعةٌ فَليسَ اللَّيلُ موجوداً ؛ سالبة ، قاله المحشّي، وكتب البعضُ أنَّ «أل» في الحكم: : للعهد، والمعهود قول المصنف : إن حكم فيها بسلب نسبةٍ على تقديرِ أُخرى، من أنَّ تعريف السالبة المستفاد من كلامِهِ؛ أي: الحاكمة بنفي الاتصال منزل عليها بجميع أقسامها الأربعة؛ أي: سالبةِ الطَّرفين أو موجبتهما، أو سالبة أحدهما موجبةِ الأُخرى، كما أنَّ الموجبة؛ أي: الحاكمة بثبوتِ الاتصال كذلكَ، وليس تَعريفُ السَّالبةِ مُنزلاً على مثل : إِنْ كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعةٌ ؛ فليسَ اللَّيلُ موجوداً ؛ لأنَّ السَّلْبَ الَّذي هو أحدُ الطَّرفَيْن لا حكم فيه عاما على ما حقَّقَ المصنِّفُ والسَّيِّدُ، فظهر أنَّ كلامَ الشَّارِحِ تَحقيق لكلام المصنِّف ؛ لَا مَا وهم فيه بعضُهُم .
Section: On the divisions of the conditional. It is an expression of the connection between the two relations. So the judgement of its negation is an expression of the negation of the connection. The negative proposition is that in which you judge the negation of the connection, not the connection of negation. For what is judged with the connection of negation is affirmative, not negative. Al-Dasuqi: And that because the relation which is judged by its affirmation or its negation upon another condition, is either necessary or perpetual or actual or possible, and he did not mention them here, being content with their mention in categoricals. (His statement: 'expression of the connection') i.e., the attachment and implication, and his statement: 'between the two relations' i.e., between the two affairs, i.e., the content of the consequent and the antecedent. (His statement: 'so the judgement of its negation...') so its negation is... etc. (His statement: 'by negation of connection') i.e., the existence of night is not necessary upon the rising of the sun. So connection here is implication, and it is not meant that the consequent is connected to the first, i.e., there is no separation between them. Al-Attar: (His statement: 'so the judgement of its negation is...') i.e., the judgement of negating a relation upon another condition... etc., and his purpose in this is to repel what is mistakenly understood from the author's statement that something like: 'If the sun is rising, then the night is not existent' is negative. The commentator said this, and some wrote that 'al' in 'al-hukm' is for covenant, and the covenanted is the author's statement: 'if it judged with negating a relation upon another condition', that the definition of the negative derived from his statement is applied to all its four divisions; i.e., both extremes negative or both affirmative, or one negative and the other affirmative, just as the affirmative, i.e., that which judges with the affirmation of connection, is likewise; and the definition of the negative is not applied to something like 'If the sun is rising, then the night is not existent' because the negation which is one of the extremes has no judgement in the whole, according to what the author and al-Sayyid verified. So it appears that the commentator's statement is a verification of the author's statement, not what some mistakenly understood.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the types of conditional propositions. The author first gives a concise definition: a conditional proposition (shartiyya) is an expression of the connection (ittisal) between two relations (nisbatayn). The affirmation of that connection makes an affirmative conditional; the judgement of its negation makes a negative conditional. The key is that negation must be of the connection itself, not a connection of a negation. The commentators expand on this. Al-Dasuqi adds that the relation (nisba) that is judged—either affirmed or negated—can be necessary, perpetual, actual, or possible; these modalities are not repeated here because they were discussed for categoricals. He also clarifies that 'connection' here means implication (luzum) or attachment, not spatial contiguity. Al-Attar restates the purpose: to repel the misunderstanding that a conditional like 'If the sun rises, then night is not existent' is negative. He explains that the definition of a negative conditional is applied to all four combinations of affirmative/negative extremes, but this example is still affirmative because the negation is only in one extreme, not a negation of the connection. The commentator's clarification confirms the author's point, contrary to what some had mistakenly thought.

Key Terms
شرطية (shartiyya): A conditional proposition (conjunctive or disjunctive) that expresses a connection between two ratios.
اتصال (ittisal): The relational connection (implication or coincidence) between the antecedent and consequent in a conjunctive conditional. [Note: In other contexts, ittisal can mean conjunction or continuity; here specifically logical implication.]

Distinguishing Negative from Affirmative Conditionals: The Locus of Negation
Author defines negative conditional as salb al-ittisal and affirmative as ittisal al-salb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional proposition is negative (salb al-ittisal) or affirmative (ittisal al-salb) and explain the logical difference.

Core Idea
A negative conditional negates the nexus; an affirmative links the antecedent to a negated consequent.

In classical Islamic logic, the conditional proposition (shartiyya muttasila) expresses a nexus (ittisal) between two propositions. But where does the negation fall? This passage from the Tadhheeb clarifies a common confusion: negating the consequent does not make a conditional negative. The key is whether the nexus itself is negated. Let's explore this distinction with precision, as it is essential for understanding legal and theological conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Nexus
Identify the two propositions: the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). The nexus is the relation of implication (luzum) between them. For example, in 'If it is wine, then it is haram,' the nexus is the connection between wine and impermissibility.
2
Find the Negation
Check if the negation (salb) is placed on the entire nexus or only on the consequent. If the negation precedes the entire conditional (e.g., 'It is not the case that if...'), it negates the nexus — that is a negative conditional. If the negation is inside the consequent (e.g., 'If..., then not...'), it affirms a nexus to a negated consequent — that is an affirmative conditional.
3
Apply the Definition
For each conditional, ask: 'Does this judge the negation of the nexus (salb al-ittisal) or the nexus of a negation (ittisal al-salb)?' The former is negative, the latter affirmative. Test with the examples from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ. عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ الْاتِّصَالِ بَيْنَ النِّسْبَتَيْنِ، فَالْحُكْمُ بِنَفْيِهَا يَكُونُ عِبَارَةً عَنْ سَلْبِ الْاتِّصَالِ. فَالْقَضِيَّةُ السَّالِبَةُ هِيَ تُحْكَمُ فِيهَا بِسَلْبِ الْاتِّصَالِ، لَا بِاتِّصَالِ السَّلْبِ، فَإِنَّ مَا حُكِمَ فِيهِ بِاتِّصَالِ السَّلْبِ مُوجِبَةٌ لَا سَالِبَةٌ. فَإِذَا قُلْنَا: لَيْسَ إِنْ كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةً؛ فَاللَّيْلُ مَوْجُودٌ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةً؛ لِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِيهَا بِسَلْبِ الْاتِّصَالِ. وَإِذَا قُلْنَا: إِنْ كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةً؛ فَلَيْسَ اللَّيْلُ مَوْجُوداً كَانَتْ مُوجِبَةً؛ لِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِيهَا بِاتِّصَالِ السَّلْبِ.
Section: On the divisions of the conditional. It is an expression of the nexus between two propositions. So the judgment negating it is an expression of the negation of the nexus. Thus the negative proposition is one in which you judge the negation of the nexus, not the nexus of negation. Indeed, that in which you judge the nexus of negation is affirmative, not negative. So if we say: 'It is not the case that if the sun is rising, then the night exists' – it is negative, because the judgment in it is of the negation of the nexus. And if we say: 'If the sun is rising, then the night does not exist' – it is affirmative, because the judgment in it is of the nexus of negation.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage defines the negative and affirmative conditional (shartiyya muttasila) by distinguishing the locus of negation. The author begins by stating the topic: the divisions of the conditional. The conditional expresses a nexus (ittisal) between two propositions – the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). When we negate the entire conditional, the judgment is the negation of that nexus (salb al-ittisal). Therefore, a negative proposition (qadiyya saliba) is one that judges the negation of the nexus, not the nexus of negation. For example, in Islamic legal reasoning: 'It is not the case that if it is wine (khamr), then it is permissible (halal)' is a negative conditional because it denies the nexus between wine and permissibility. Conversely, a conditional that judges the nexus of negation – such as 'If it is wine, then it is not permissible' – is affirmative, because it affirms a nexus between wine and the negation of permissibility. The author illustrates with the sun and night: 'It is not that if the sun is rising, then the night exists' is negative; 'If the sun is rising, then the night does not exist' is affirmative. The key point: the location of the negation matters. If the negation appears in the consequent, the overall conditional is still affirmative because it affirms a nexus between the antecedent and a negated consequent. The classic error is to mistake 'If P, then not Q' as a negative conditional. The author corrects this by emphasizing that a negative conditional negates the nexus itself, not the consequent.

Key Terms
قضية سالبة
سلب الاتصال
اتصال السلب

D) If a person is a scholar, he is knowledgeable.

Key Takeaway
A negative conditional negates the nexus itself, not the consequent; a conditional with a negated consequent is still affirmative.

Luzumiyya vs Ittifaqiyya: The Two Types of Connected Conditionals
Author defines luzumiyya conditional as having a relation between antecedent and consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya conditionals by identifying whether the connection is based on a real relationship (al-alaqa) or mere coincidence, using examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A conditional is luzumiyya only if there is a real relationship that necessitates the connection, not mere coincidence.

In this lesson, we examine the classification of connected conditionals (al-muttasila) into luzumiyya (implicative) and ittifaqiyya (coincidental). The key criterion is whether the connection between antecedent and consequent is grounded in an inherent relationship (al-alaqa) or not. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the validity of conditional propositions in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In the conditional proposition, locate the part after 'if' (the antecedent, muqaddam) and the part after 'then' (the consequent, tali). For example, in 'If the sun rises, then night is not present', the antecedent is 'the sun rises' and the consequent is 'night is not present'.
2
Determine the basis of the connection
Ask: Is the connection between antecedent and consequent due to a real relationship (like causality or logical entailment) or merely due to coincidence in reality? If the connection holds in every instance because of an inherent link, it is a real relationship.
3
Classify as luzumiyya or ittifaqiyya
If the connection is based on a real relationship, the conditional is luzumiyya (implicative). If it is based on mere coincidence (e.g., 'If Zayd is in the market, then Umar is at home' without any necessary link), it is ittifaqiyya (coincidental).
4
Test with Islamic examples
Apply this classification to examples from Islamic sciences. For instance, 'If a person performs Hajj with valid intention, then the Hajj is accepted' is luzumiyya because valid intention is a condition for acceptance in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المتّصلة سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة، إما لزومية إن كان ذلك الحكم بالاتصال أو سلبه لعلاقة بين المقدّم والتّالي، كالمثالين المذكورين. فإن الحكم بالاتصال أو سلبه فيهما ليس لمجرّد اتّفاق المقدّم والتّالي في الواقع، بل لعلاقة بينهما توجب ذلك. والمراد بالعلاقة ما بسببه يستلزم المقدّم التالي.
Then the connected [conditional], whether affirmative or negative, is either luzumiyya (implicative) if that judgment of connection or its negation is due to a relationship between the antecedent and the consequent, like the two examples mentioned. For the judgment of connection or its negation in them is not merely due to coincidence of the antecedent and consequent in reality, but rather due to a relationship between them that necessitates that. And by 'relationship' is meant that because of which the antecedent entails the consequent.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of connected conditionals (al-muttasila). After establishing that conditionals can be affirmative or negative, the author classifies them further based on whether the connection between antecedent and consequent is necessary (luzumiyya) or merely coincidental (ittifaqiyya). The focus here is on luzumiyya, which is defined as a conditional where the judgment (either affirming or denying connection) is made because of a real relationship (al-alaqa) between the parts, not just because they happen to coincide in reality. The two examples alluded to are earlier in the text: 'If the sun rises, then night is not present' (negative) and 'If the sun rises, then it is not the case that night is absent' (affirmative). In both, the connection derives from the causal link between sunrise and the absence of night. The author clarifies that the relationship means something that causes the antecedent to entail the consequent. In Islamic sciences, similar conditionals are used, e.g., 'If someone performs Hajj with valid intention, then the Hajj is accepted' – the connection is based on the rule that valid intention (niyya) is a condition for acceptance, so there is a real relationship.

لزومية
علاقة
مقدّم
تالي

Key Takeaway
The essential criterion for a luzumiyya conditional is the presence of a real relationship ('alaqa) between antecedent and consequent that necessitates the connection, distinguishing it from the ittifaqiyya type which is based on coincidence.

Conditional Propositions: Luzumiyya vs Ittifaqiyya
Author defines conditional proposition and distinguishes luzumiyya from ittifaqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a luzumiyya and an ittifaqiyya conditional by identifying whether a known relationship ('alāqa) exists between antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
A conditional is not about factual co-occurrence but about a known relationship that necessitates the consequent.

In this section, the author defines the conditional proposition and clarifies that the connection between antecedent and consequent is not based on mere coincidence. Instead, a real relationship ('alāqa) must be known to the knower for the conditional to be considered implicative (luzumiyya). Without such a known relationship, the conditional is merely coincidental (ittifaqiyya). This distinction is essential for evaluating logical implications in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In any conditional proposition, locate the part following 'if' (the antecedent) and the part following 'then' (the consequent). In the Arabic text, these are called al-muqaddam and al-tālī.
2
Check for a known relationship (alāqa)
Determine whether there is a known cause, law, or reason that, given the truth of the antecedent, necessitates the truth of the consequent. This must be known to the logician or the one making the statement.
The passage states: 'if they came to know something that necessitates the truth of the consequent on the supposition of its truth' – this is the alāqa.
3
Classify the conditional
If such a known relationship exists, the conditional is luzumiyya (implicative). If not, it is ittifaqiyya (coincidental). The truth of the antecedent and consequent in reality is insufficient.
Example: 'If the sun rises, then day exists' is luzumiyya because the causal link is known. 'If fire exists, then a cat is on the mat' is ittifaqiyya unless a connection is known.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the fiqh example: 'If water is two qullas, then it is pure.' The relationship (the quantity as a cause for purity) is known from hadith. Thus, this conditional is luzumiyya.
The author emphasizes that coexistence is possible but must have a cause; the distinction depends on whether that cause is known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ
لَيْسَ لِمُجَرَّدِ اتِّفَاقِ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَالتَّالِي فِي الْوَاقِعِ، بَلْ لِعَلَاقَةٍ بَيْنَهُمَا تُوجِبُ ذَلِكَ .
وَالْمُرَادُ بِالْعَلَاقَةِ مَا بِسَبَبِهِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْمُقَدَّمُ التَّالِي .
قَالَ الْمُصَنِّفُ : وَالتَّحْقِيقُ أَنَّ الْمَعِيَّةَ فِي الْوُجُودِ أَمْرٌ مُمْكِنٌ وَلَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِنْ عِلَّةٍ تَقْتَضِيهِ، إِلَّا أَنَّهُمْ لَمَّا لَاحَظُوا الْمُقَدَّمَ؛ فَإِنِ اطَّلَعُوا عَلَى أَمْرٍ يَقْتَضِي صِدْقَ التَّالِي عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ صِدْقِهِ وَاعْتَبَرُوا ذَلِكَ الْأَمْرَ ; سَمُّوا الْمُتَّصِلَةَ لُزُومِيَّةً، وَإِلَّا ; فَاتِّفَاقِيَّةً .
Section: On the divisions of the conditional [proposition]. It is not due to mere coincidence of the antecedent and consequent in reality, but due to a relationship between them that necessitates that. And what is meant by 'relationship' is that by which the antecedent necessitates the consequent. The author said: The verification is that coexistence in existence is a possible matter, and it must have a cause that necessitates it, except that when they considered the antecedent—if they came to know something that necessitates the truth of the consequent on the supposition of its truth and they considered that matter, they called the conditional [proposition] 'implicative'; otherwise, 'coincidental.'
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional proposition (al-shartiyya) and divides it into two types: luzumiyya (implicative) and ittifaqiyya (coincidental). The author begins by stating that the judgment of connection (ittisal) or disconnection (infisal) in a conditional is not based merely on the factual co-occurrence of the antecedent and consequent in reality. Rather, it must be grounded in a relationship (’alaqa) that necessitates the connection or its negation. The term 'relationship' is then defined as that by which the antecedent (muqaddam) entails the consequent (tali). This is the core condition for a conditional to be considered implicative. The author further explains that coexistence (ma’iyya) in existence is possible but requires a cause. However, logicians distinguish between the two types based on whether the cause is known or not: if they perceive a cause that, given the truth of the antecedent, necessitates the truth of the consequent, they call the conditional 'luzumiyya'; if not, they call it 'ittifaqiyya'. This distinction is crucial for understanding logical implication in the Islamic tradition, where examples like 'If the sun rises, then day exists' illustrate a clear causal relationship (hence luzumiyya), whereas accidental co-occurrences without a known link are ittifaqiyya. The passage establishes that the relationship must be known to the knower for the conditional to be considered implicative.

Key Terms
شَرْطِيَّة
لُزُومِيَّة
اتِّفَاقِيَّة
عَلاقَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a luzumiyya conditional from an ittifaqiyya conditional?
A) The truth of the antecedent and consequent in reality.
B) The presence of a known relationship that necessitates the consequent from the antecedent.
C) The conditional being affirmative or negative.
D) The antecedent being a cause of the consequent always.

Key Takeaway
A conditional is classified as luzumiyya only when there is a known relationship that necessitates the consequent; otherwise it is ittifaqiyya.

The Coincidental Conditional: Definition and Distinction
Author defines ittifaqiyya, explains its relation to 'illa, and provides an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a coincidental conditional from an implicational conditional by identifying whether the judgment is based on an observed logical relation.

Core Idea
Every coexistence has a cause; in the coincidental conditional, the cause is hidden, not absent.

Conditional propositions are divided into two main types: the implicational (luzumiyya) and the coincidental (ittifaqiyya). The difference lies in the role of the 'illa (relation) between the antecedent and consequent. In this lesson, we will explore the definition of ittifaqiyya as given by the author and the important refinement by al-Dasuqi, who clarifies that a cause always exists but may be hidden. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing conditional statements in Islamic logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditionals
Read the opening sentence: 'If the judgment of connection or its negation is not due to a relation, but merely due to the coincidence of the antecedent and consequent, then it is coincidental.' Note the contrast with implicational conditionals where the judgment is based on a clear relation.
The relation referred to is the 'illa (cause) that makes the consequent follow from the antecedent.
2
Understand the role of the 'illa in each type
Al-Dasuqi explains that even in the coincidental conditional, there is a cause for the coexistence, but it is not apparent and not known. In the implicational conditional, the cause is clearly actualized and the judgment is based on observing it.
This refines the initial definition: the cause is not absent, just unobserved.
3
Apply the distinction to the commentator's statement
The commentator says 'and if it is not due to a relation' – meaning not due to observing the relation. Recognize that the judgment in ittifaqiyya is not made in consideration of the hidden cause.
This subtle point prevents the misconception that ittifaqiyya has no cause at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ (وَإِلَّا) وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنِ الْحُكْمُ بِالْاتِّصَالِ أَوْ سَلْبِهِ لِعَلَاقَةٍ، بَلْ يَكُونُ لِمُجَرَّدِ اتِّفَاقِ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَالتَّالِي؛ (فَاتِّفَاقِيَّةٌ). قَالَ الدَّسُوقِيُّ: وَبِعِبَارَةٍ أُخْرَى: اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمَعِيَّةَ أَمْرٌ مُمْكِنٌ لَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِنْ عِلَّةٍ، فَفِي الْاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ أَيْضًا الْعَلَاقَةُ الْمُقْتَضِيَةُ لِلِاجْتِمَاعِ مُتَحَقِّقَةٌ، لَكِنَّهَا غَيْرُ ظَاهِرَةٍ وَغَيْرُ مَعْلُومَةٍ، فَلَيْسَ الْحُكْمُ فِيهَا لِمُلَاحَظَةِ عَلَاقَةٍ بِخِلَافِ اللُّزُومِيَّةِ، فَإِنَّ الْعَلَاقَةَ فِيهَا ظَاهِرَةُ التَّحَقُّقِ، فَالْحُكْمُ فِيهَا لِمُلَاحَظَتِهَا، فَقَوْلُ الشَّارِحِ: وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لِعَلَاقَةٍ؛ أَيْ: لِمُلَاحَظَتِهَا. (قَوْلُهُ: إِنْ كَانَ الْإِنْسَانُ نَاطِقًا؛ فَالْحِمَارُ نَاهِقٌ) أَيِ اتَّفَقَ أَنَّهُ عِنْدَ نُطْقِ الْإِنْسَانِ نَهَقَ الْحِمَارُ، فَنَهِيقُهُ حَاصِلٌ عِنْدَ نُطْقِ الْإِنْسَانِ؛ أَيْ: اتَّفَقَا فِي سَاعَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ مَثَلًا. وَيَدُلُّ عَلَى ذَلِكَ أَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ عَدَمِ أَحَدِهِمَا عَدَمُ الْآخَرِ، وَلَا مِنْ وُجُودِ أَحَدِهِمَا وُجُودُ الْآخَرِ، وَاللَّازِمُ فِي اللُّزُومِيَّةِ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ عَدَمِهِ عَدَمُ الْمَلْزُومِ، وَالْمَلْزُومُ فِيهَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ وُجُودُ اللَّازِمِ، وَاللَّازِمُ هُوَ التَّالِي، وَالْمَلْزُومُ هُوَ الْمُقَدَّمُ.
Section: On the divisions of the conditional. (Otherwise) And if the judgment of connection or its negation is not due to a relation, but is merely due to the coincidence of the antecedent and consequent, then (coincidental). Al-Dasuqi said: In other words, know that coexistence is a possible matter that must have a cause. So in the coincidental (conditional) as well, the relation necessitating the combination is actualized, but it is not apparent and not known. So the judgment in it is not due to the observation of a relation, contrary to the implicational (conditional), for the relation in it is clearly actualized, so the judgment in it is due to its observation. Thus the commentator's statement: 'and if it is not due to a relation' means: not due to observing it. (His statement: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying') means it coincidentally happened that at the time of man's speech, the donkey brayed; so its braying occurs at the time of man's speech, i.e., they coincide in one hour, for example. That is indicated by the fact that the non-existence of one does not necessitate the non-existence of the other, nor does the existence of one necessitate the existence of the other, while the necessary (consequent) in the implicational (conditional) necessitates from its non-existence the non-existence of the necessitating (antecedent), and the necessitating in it necessitates from its existence the existence of the necessary; the necessary is the consequent, and the necessitating is the antecedent.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage defines the coincidental conditional (al-shartiyya al-ittifaqiyya) and contrasts it with the implicational conditional (al-shartiyya al-luzumiyya). The author begins by stating that if the judgment of connection (or disconnection) between antecedent and consequent is not due to a relation ('illa), but merely due to the accidental coexistence of the two parts, then it is called 'ittifaqiyya'. Al-Dasuqi then elaborates: every coexistence must have a cause, so even in the coincidental conditional there is a relation that brings the two parts together, but it is hidden and not observed. Therefore, the judgment is not made in consideration of that relation, whereas in the implicational conditional the relation is clear and the judgment is based on observing it. So when the commentator says 'not due to a relation', he means 'not due to observing the relation'. The example given is: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.' This is a coincidental conditional because there is no real relation between man's rationality and the donkey's braying; rather, they happen to occur at the same time. The passage then explains the logical behavior: in a coincidental conditional, the non-existence of one does not imply the non-existence of the other, and the existence of one does not imply the existence of the other. This is opposed to the implicational conditional, where the consequent (lazim) follows from the antecedent (malzum) such that if the consequent is absent, the antecedent is absent, and if the antecedent is present, the consequent is present. This distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing conditional propositions in logical reasoning.

Key Terms
شَرْطِيَّة
اتِّفَاقِيَّة
لُزُومِيَّة
عَلَاقَة

Checkpoint
According to al-Dasuqi's commentary, what is the difference between a coincidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) and an implicational conditional (luzumiyya)?
A) In ittifaqiyya, there is no relation at all between antecedent and consequent; in luzumiyya, there is a clear relation.
B) In ittifaqiyya, the relation exists but is hidden and not observed; in luzumiyya, the relation is clearly observed and the judgment is based on it.
C) In ittifaqiyya, the antecedent and consequent are unrelated; in luzumiyya, they are necessarily related.
D) In ittifaqiyya, the judgment is based on the relation; in luzumiyya, it is not.

Key Takeaway
The coincidental conditional has a hidden cause for the coexistence of its parts, but the judgment is made without observing that cause, unlike the implicational conditional where the cause is manifest and judgment is based on it.

Analyzing the Example of the Coincidental Conditional
Author gives example of ittifaqiyya and explains its logical behavior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a conditional is coincidental by checking the independence of its antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
In a coincidental conditional, the antecedent can be true without the consequent, and vice versa.

After defining the coincidental conditional, the author provides a concrete example: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.' This example illustrates the logical behavior of an ittifaqiyya: the truth of one part does not guarantee the truth of the other, nor does the falsity of one imply the falsity of the other. In this lesson, we will dissect this example and contrast it with an implicational conditional to internalize the test for coincidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In the example 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying', the antecedent is 'man is rational' and the consequent is 'the donkey is braying'. These are two completely unrelated facts that happen to coincide temporally.
2
Test the logical entailments
Check if the existence of the antecedent implies the existence of the consequent. Clearly, man's rationality does not cause a donkey to bray. Similarly, the non-existence of the antecedent (no rational man) does not imply the non-existence of the consequent (donkey might bray anyway).
This independence is the hallmark of ittifaqiyya.
3
Contrast with an implicational conditional
Consider an implicational conditional like 'If the fire is hot, then burning occurs'. Here, the presence of heat necessarily leads to burning (under normal conditions). Absence of burning implies absence of heat. This shows the necessary relation absent in the coincidental case.
The author states: 'the non-existence of one does not necessitate the non-existence of the other' for ittifaqiyya.
4
Apply the test to new examples
Given any conditional, ask: Does the antecedent have a clear causal or logical relation to the consequent? If not, it is likely coincidental. Use the test of independence: can the antecedent be true while the consequent is false, and vice versa?
This test is derived from the author's description of the logical behavior.

5 min
The Coincidental Conditional (Ittifaqiyya)
Author defines the coincidental conditional with affirmative and negative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a coincidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) and distinguish it from a necessary conditional (luzumiyya) by checking whether there is a genuine relation between antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
In a coincidental conditional, the connection is accidental—no real relation ties the two parts together.

This lesson introduces the coincidental conditional, a subtype of conjunctive conditional propositions. In logic, conditionals usually express a dependency, but sometimes the connection is merely coincidental. We will examine the definition and two examples from the text to master this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find where the author describes the ittifaqiyya. Note that it involves judgment of connection without a relation ('illa).
The definition appears in the affirmative example: 'If man is rational, then the donkey brays.'
2
Examine the affirmative example
Look at 'If man is rational, then the donkey brays'. Why is there no relation? Because rationality of humans and braying of donkeys have no logical or causal tie. The connection is simply that both happen to be true.
3
Examine the negative example
The example 'Not at all, if this is black, then he is a writer' for a black non-writer shows what happens when the antecedent is true but consequent false; the conditional is false because the agreement is negated.
This illustrates the negative coincidental conditional.
4
Contrast with luzumiyya
A luzumiyya conditional has a real relation (causal, natural, or legal). If you can identify a genuine relation, it is not ittifaqiyya.
For instance, 'If the sun rises, then it is day' is luzumiyya due to the causal relation.
5
Apply the test
For a given conditional, ask: Is there a necessary connection (by reason, custom, or law) between the antecedent and consequent? If no, it is coincidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كقولنا : إن كانَ الإنسانُ ناطقاً ; فالحمارُ ناهق في الموجِبَةِ، فإنَّه حكم فيها بالاتصال، لكن لا لعلاقة؛ إذ لا علاقة بين ناطِقِيَّةِ الإنسانِ وناهقية الحمار، بل لمجرَّدِ اتِّفَاقِ الطَّرَفَينِ وصِدْقِهِما في الواقع؛ لأنَّهما وُجِدَا كذلك. فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ. وكقولنا للأسْوَدِ اللاكاتبِ : «ليس أَلْبَتَّةَ إذ كان هذا أسود؛ فهو كاتب» في السالبة. فالاتفاقيَّةُ الموجبة هي التي حكم فيها بثبوتِ الاتفاقِ، (قَوْلُهُ : لَيْسَ أَلْبَيَّةَ) أي : فَالاتِّفاقُ حصلَ بينَ كونِهِ أسود، وكونه غير كاتب، ونفي الاتفاق بين كونه أسود وكاتب . (قَوْلُهُ بِتُبُوتِ الاِتِّفَاقِ أَي بِاتِّصالِ التَّالي بالمقدَّم؛ بالنظر للاتفاق
Like our saying: 'If man is rational, then the donkey brays' in the affirmative, for judgment of connection is made therein, but not due to a relation, since there is no relation between man's rationality and the donkey's braying, but merely due to agreement of the two parts and their truth in reality, because they are found so. Section: On the divisions of the conditional. And like our saying concerning the black who is not a writer: 'Not at all, if this is black, then he is a writer' in the negative. So the affirmative coincidental is that in which the establishment of agreement is judged. His saying: 'Not at all' means the agreement occurred between his being black and his being non-writer, and negation of agreement between his being black and writer. His saying 'by the establishment of agreement' means by the connection of the consequent to the antecedent with regard to the agreement.
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage defines the coincidental conditional (al-muttasila al-ittifaqiyya), a subtype of conjunctive conditional propositions. The author provides two examples: an affirmative and a negative, along with explanation. The affirmative example is "If man is rational, then the donkey brays." The judgment of connection is made, but not because there is any real relation between rationality and braying; rather, the two parts merely happen to be true together in reality. The negative example is "Not at all, if this is black, then he is a writer" meaning there is no agreement between being black and being a writer. The commentator clarifies that the agreement is between the antecedent and the negation of the consequent, or simply that the judgment is based on the mere fact of the consequent being true (or false) when the antecedent is true, without any logical dependency. Thus, the ittifaqiyya is defined by the absence of a cause ('illa) connecting the two parts, unlike the luzumiyya where a relation exists.

Key Terms
الاتفاقية (al-ittifāqiyya)
المقدَّم (al-muqaddam)
التَّالي (al-tālī)
القضية الشرطية المتصلة (al-qaḍiyya al-sharṭiyya al-muttaṣila)

A) If the sun rises, then it is day.

Key Takeaway
The coincidental conditional is defined by the absence of any necessary relation between its two parts; their connection is only due to factual agreement.

Accidental vs Implicative Conditionals: Definitions and Their Revision
Author defines accidental/implicative conditionals and revises to include false propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between accidental and implicative conditionals and explain how the revised definition expands to include false propositions.

Core Idea
The accidental conditional rests on mere truth coincidence; the implicative on causal relation—and both definitions must cover false judgments too.

In Islamic logic, conditionals are divided into accidental and implicative types. The key difference lies in whether the connection between antecedent and consequent is based on a real relation or merely on a coincidence in truth. This passage from the commentary on Tadhheeb al-Mantiq presents the definitions and an important revision that extends them to cover false as well as true propositions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing the logical structure of conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional proposition
Locate the 'if...then' structure in a given statement. For example, 'If this is black, then it is a writer.' Note the antecedent ('this is black') and consequent ('it is a writer').
The conditional can be positive or negative.
2
Determine the basis of connection
Ask: Is there a causal or real implicative relation (luzum) between antecedent and consequent? Or does the truth of the consequent merely happen to coincide with the truth of the antecedent (ittifaq)? The implicative type involves a relation; the accidental type does not.
The passage emphasizes that in accidental, there is no relation; in implicative, one is a cause of the other.
3
Check the judgment's correspondence to reality
According to the revised definition, the judgment of connection (whether accidental or implicative) can be false. If the conditional is false, it is still classified by the type of connection claimed. For instance, 'If this is black, then it is a writer' is a false accidental conditional if the person is not a writer.
Original definitions only applied to true conditionals; the revision covers both.
4
Apply the distinction to a real example from Islamic law
Consider a conditional in a contract: 'If you pay the price, ownership transfers.' This is implicative because payment causes transfer. Compare with 'If you wear black, then today is Friday,' which is accidental unless there is a known relation.
This distinction helps avoid confusing coincidences with legal causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطيَّةِ وكقولنا للأسْوَدِ اللاكاتبِ : «ليس أَلْبَتَّةَ إذ كان هذا أسود؛ فهو كاتب» في السالبة . فالاتفاقيَّةُ الموجبة هى التي حكم فيها بثبوتِ الاتفاقِ، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : لَيْسَ أَلْبَيَّةَ) أي : فَالاتِّفاقُ حصلَ بينَ كونِهِ أسود، وكونه غير كاتب، ونفي الاتفاق بين كونه أسود وكاتب . (قَوْلُهُ بِتُبُوتِ الاِتِّفَاقِ أَي بِاتِّصالِ التَّالي بالمقدَّم؛ بالنظر للاتفاق العطار صدقِ المقدَّمِ ؛ لَا لِعلاقة بلْ لمجرَّدِ صدقِ التّالي، ويجوز أنْ يكونَ المقدَّمُ فيها صادقاً وكاذباً، وتُسمى بهذا المعنى : اتِّفاقيَّةً عامةً، وبالمعنى الأَوَّلِ : اتفاقيَّةً خاصةً للعموم والخصوص بينَهُما ، فإنَّه متى صدق المقدَّم والتّالي؛ فقد صدق التالي ولا ينعكس ا.هـ. قال العصام : والاتفاقيَّةُ العامَّةُ تُستعمل في القياسات الخلفية، وفي محاورات اللغة للمبالغة في وقوع التّالي، ومنها : أما بعد في ديباجات الكتب ا.هـ. واعلَمْ أنَّ المصنف عدل عن قول الأصل في تعريف اللزوميَّةِ والاتفاقية بقولِهِ في الأُولَى : هي التي صدقَ التَّالي فيها على تقديرِ صِدْقِ المقدَّم لعلاقة بينهما توجبُ ذلك، وفي الثَّانيةِ : هي التي يكون ذلك فيها لمجرَّدِ توافق الجزأين على الصدقِ لِشمولِ تَعريفِهِ الصَّادقة والكاذبة فيها ، بخلافِ تعريف الأصل؛ فإِنَّهُ مُختص بالصَّادِقِ منهما، ولذلك قال في شرحِهِ لِلأصل : إِنَّ التَّعريفَ الشَّامِلَ لِلصَّادِقِ والكاذب هو أنَّ اللُّزومِيَّةَ ما حكم فيها بصدقِ الثَّالي على تقديرِ صدق المقدَّم لعلاقة بينهما، والاتفاقيَّةَ : ما حكم فيها بذلك بمجرَّدِ توافقِهما على الصَّدقِ مِن غير علاقةٍ أو من غير اعتبارها، فإنْ كانَ الحُكْمُ مُطابقاً ؛ فَصادقةٌ، وإلا ؛ فكاذبة، قال عبد الحكيم : وعدم شمولِ التَّعريف للكاذبة منهما بناءً على أنَّ المتبادر من قولنا : وهي التي صدقَ التَّالي فيها على تقديرِ صِدْقِ المقدَّمِ ؛ أنْ يكونَ ذلكَ في نفس الأمرِ وَلَو أُريدَ بِهِ أَنْ يكون ذلك مفهوماً ومدلولاً لها ؛ سواء طابق الواقع أم لا ، شملَ الكاذبة أيضاً . (قَوْلُهُ : وَكَقَوْلِنَا لِلْأَسْوَدِ... إِلَخ) ليسَتِ اللَّامُ لِلتَّبليغ كَمَا يُقالُ : قلتُ لِزِيدٍ كذا ، بل هي بمعنى في أيّ، وكَقولنا في حقّ الأسود. والسالبة هي التي حكم فيها بسلب الاتفاق. وكذا اللزومية الموجبَةُ، حكم فيها بثبوتِ اللزوم، والسَّالبةُ حُكِمَ فيها بسلب اللُّزوم . (قَوْلُهُ: بِتُبُوتِ اللُّزُوْمِ أي: باتِّصالِ التَّالي بالمقدم؛ بالنظر للزوم بينهما ؛ لكون أحدهما علَّةً في الآخر، وَكَذَا يُقالُ في جانبِ السَّلْبِ.
Section: On the divisions of the conditional. And like our saying about the non-writer black: 'It is absolutely not that if this is black, then it is a writer' in the negative. So the positive accidental is that in which the agreement is judged to be established. Al-Dasuqi: His saying 'not absolutely' means: the agreement occurs between its being black and its being non-writer, and the negation of the agreement between its being black and writer. His saying 'with the establishment of the agreement' means with the connection of the consequent to the antecedent with respect to the agreement. Al-Attar: The truth of the antecedent, not due to a relation but merely due to the truth of the consequent, and it is possible that the antecedent in it be true or false, and it is called in this sense general accidental, and in the first sense specific accidental, there being generality and specificity between them. For whenever the antecedent and consequent are true, then the consequent is true, but not conversely. Al-Isam said: The general accidental is used in circular syllogisms, and in linguistic conversations for exaggeration in the occurrence of the consequent, among which is 'as for after' in the prefaces of books. Know that the author departed from the statement of the original in defining the implicative and accidental by his saying in the first: it is that in which the consequent is true on the assumption of the truth of the antecedent due to a relation between them that necessitates that, and in the second: it is that in which that occurs merely by the agreement of the two parts in truth, to include in his definition the true and false ones therein, unlike the definition of the original, for it is specific to the true one of them. Therefore he said in his commentary on the original: The comprehensive definition for the true and false is that the implicative is that in which the truth of the consequent is judged on the assumption of the truth of the antecedent due to a relation between them, and the accidental is that in which that is judged merely by their agreement in truth without a relation or without considering it. If the judgment corresponds, then it is true, otherwise false. Abd al-Hakim said: The lack of inclusivity of the definition for the false one of them is based on the fact that the understood from our saying 'it is that in which the consequent is true on the assumption of the truth of the antecedent' is that it is in reality; and if by it is meant that it is understood and indicated by it, whether it corresponds to reality or not, it would also include the false one. (His saying: 'And like our saying about the black... etc.') The lam is not for communication as it is said 'I said to Zaid such', but rather it is in the sense of 'concerning', i.e., our saying concerning the black. The negative is that in which the agreement is judged to be negated. Similarly the positive implicative is that in which the implicative relation is judged to be established, and the negative is that in which the implicative relation is judged to be negated. (His saying: 'With the establishment of the implicative relation') means with the connection of the consequent to the antecedent with respect to an implicative relation between them, because one of them is a cause in the other, and similarly is said regarding the side of negation.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage is a definitional passage from a commentary on a logic text. It begins with a section heading on the divisions of the conditional proposition. The author provides an example for the negative accidental conditional: 'It is absolutely not that if this is black, then it is a writer' — this illustrates a case where the antecedent 'this is black' and consequent 'it is a writer' (for a non-writer black person) have no real connection, only an accidental agreement in truth (both false in this case). Then the definition of the positive accidental conditional is given: it is a conditional in which the judgment is that the agreement (i.e., both parts being true together) holds. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that in the example, there is agreement between 'being black' and 'being non-writer' (both true), and negation of agreement between 'being black' and 'being writer'. Al-Attar clarifies that the judgment is based not on any causal relation but merely on the truth of the consequent coinciding with the truth of the antecedent. He notes that the antecedent can be true or false, and this broader sense is called 'general accidental', while the earlier sense (where both are actually true) is 'specific accidental'. Al-Isam adds that the general accidental is used in certain logical arguments and in rhetorical language for emphasis. The author then points out that the original writer (the matn author) revised the definitions of the implicative and accidental conditionals to include both true and false cases. The original definitions were limited to true propositions. The new definition states: an implicative conditional is where the truth of the consequent is judged on the assumption of the antecedent's truth due to a relation; an accidental conditional is where this judgment is made merely because the two parts happen to agree in truth, regardless of relation. If the judgment matches reality, it is true; otherwise false. The commentary by Abd al-Hakim notes that the original definition's restrictiveness comes from the immediate understanding that 'on the assumption of truth' refers to actual truth; if reinterpreted as merely what the proposition expresses, it would include false ones. Finally, the definitions of the negative accidental (negation of agreement) and the positive/negative implicative (affirmation/negation of a causal relation) are given, along with a note by al-Dasuqi that the implicative relation involves one being a cause of the other.

اتفاقية
لزومية
السالبة

According to the passage, what distinguishes an accidental conditional (ittifāqiyya) from an implicative conditional (lāzimiyya)?
A) The accidental conditional involves a causal relation; the implicative does not.
B) The accidental conditional is based on mere agreement in truth without a relation; the implicative involves a real relation.
C) The accidental conditional is always false; the implicative is always true.
D) The accidental conditional has only a negative form; the implicative has only a positive form.

Key Takeaway
The accidental and implicative conditionals are distinguished by whether the consequent follows due to a real relation or mere truth coincidence, and both definitions apply to true and false propositions.
